# Allegory — YouTube English Transcripts (timestamped)

## Episode 1: Noor-e-Tawheed — *Light of Oneness*

*[Watch](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=5qt5uIkKUgY) · runtime 43:43*

**[0:00:11]** Guru Nanak, the 15th century spiritual leader is a guiding force for many. He spent over two decades traveling to distant lands to engage in philosophical and social dialogues with people of diverse cultures and belief systems. Through the medium of words and music, he gracefully imparted experiential insights, fearlessly challenged the binary constructs of society, and relentlessly opposed gender, religious, racial and class inequalities.

**[0:00:53]** My name is Amardeep Singh. I retrace the life events of Guru Nanak as narrated in the earliest historical texts. He had his own message of oneness of humanity and that cut across across all religions.

**[0:01:24]** 530 years ago coming to uncharted lands but still making such a big impact. An impact which not only had permanency, which was carried on. 'Noor-e-Tawheed', the 'Light of Oneness', presents the tender years of the gentle valiant who ignited the spirit of oneness.

**[0:03:35]** 'Allegory' is a revelation of a hidden meaning within a narrative! For time immemorial, individuals have been traveling to new lands to fulfill their personal motives. Alexander conquered nations. Marco Polo explored trade opportunities.

**[0:04:06]** Guru Nanak holds a distinction. He traversed vast geographies on an altruistic purpose to create an awareness of oneness. Guru Nanak wrote nothing about his personal life but he did leave a large repository of experiential wisdom, which inspire many to remain as learners at every stage of their lives.

**[0:04:50]** The term janam sakhi comprises two words, 'Janam' meaning life and 'Sakhi' meaning narratives. This 'Janamsakhi' literature was produced to essentially preserve and spread the philosophy of Guru Nanak. Accounts of Guru Nanak's life events were passed on through oral traditions by people of faith and were later documented as texts.

**[0:05:18]** Around 65 years after Guru Nanak life, the first hagiographic account about him was ascried by Bhai Gurdas. This was followed by 'Janamsakhis, now known by the names Seehan Uppal, Hafizabad, Meherban, Bahi Mani Singh, Vilayatwali, Colebrook's and B40.

**[0:05:44]** While these texts have overlaps and variances, they are the only earliest historic sources documenting life events of Guru Nanak. Interestingly, with passage of time, newer narratives have been expanding Guru Nanak's travels to geographies not mentioned in the earliest texts. In addition, his life events have been overlaid with miracles and myths, overshadowing his core philosophy.

**[0:06:15]** In recent times, Dr. Kirpla Singh, in his study titled, 'Janamsakhi Parampara', mapped the life events of Guru Nanak by rationally analyzing the earliest hagiographies. Guru Nanak's travels, as per this assessment, cover nine regions, Pakistan India Tibet Bangladesh Sri Lanka Saudi Arabia Iraq Iran and Afghanistan.

**[0:06:50]** We travel to these regions to unravel the 'Allegory'. With a desire to understand the life and mission of Guru Nanak, we embarked on a journey, from his birthplace in Pakistan.

**[0:07:21]** 'Rai Bhoe Di Talwandi', now known as Nankana Sahib, is a town approximately 80 kilometers west of Lahore. We have reached Nankana Sahib. Nankana, The word comprises of two words 'Nanak' and 'Aana'.

**[0:07:50]** It is the coming of Nanak. This settlement was founded by a muslim landlord Rai Bhoe. Today, this region is a thriving agricultural belt but in the 15th century, it was a dense forest, nestled away from the seats of power, somewhat uninfluenced by the political and religious tumults of the region.

**[0:08:33]** We are now heading to meet the descendants of Rai Bular, who was the chieftain of this area at the time of Guru Nanak’s birth. Rai Saleem Bhatti sir.

**[0:08:56]** I would like to know a little bit about the association of your family with Guru Nanak. I am the 19th generation of 'Rai Bular Bhatti, who was the one to recognize the divine authority of Baba Guru Nanak Dev Ji Maharaj. Rai Bular Bhatti was the landlord of this area when Baba Guru Nanak Dev Ji Maharaj was born. Rai Saleem Bhatti drove us to a small settlement close to Nankana to show us an interesting connection to the past.

**[0:10:07]** Guru Nanak was born here, at Talwandi, on 15th April 1469, to Mother Tripta and Father Kalyan Das Bedi, popularly known as Mehta Kalu, the local revenue official. Several gurdwaras adorn Nankana Sahib.

**[0:11:13]** The most important of these being the Janam Asthan, the birthplace of Guru Nanak, which was earlier known as Kalu’s Kotha. This gurdwara is built in the memory of Guru Nanak's birth.

**[0:11:44]** In the partition of 1947, the subcontinent was divided into two sovereign nations, India and Pakistan. The displacement of communities based on faith, created a void in the presence of the followers of Guru Nanak in the city of Nankana. In the 1970's, Pakistani Sikh community from the Northwest region started settling in Nankana.

**[0:12:29]** Today, this city's bustling with a Sikh population, entirely of Pashtun descent. In the 'Janamsakhis' (hagiographies), it is mentioned that Pandit Hardayal had predicted Guru Nanak will become a spiritual leader for both Hindus and Muslims.

**[0:12:56]** Indeed, he grew up to become a spiritual light for the entire humanity. It is for this reason, that it is said, Guru Nanak was the younger of the two siblings. The elder was his sister, Bebe Nanaki.

**[0:13:22]** The 15th century was a turbulent period, where society was operating on the pillars of caste divisions, ritualism, oppression and hierarchy. The religious leadership was socially oppressing the weak by imposing ritualistic norms and orthodoxy. For a long period, before the birth of Guru Nanak, the Indus Region was experiencing political instability, caused by the frequent invasions led by the Central Asian chieftains.

**[0:13:58]** Under these circumstances, the natives of the Indic region were deprived of the prerogative, the freedom of thought and belief. Guru Nanak, a beacon of revolution, strived to break the shackles that had entangled the society. In close proximity to Gurdwara Janam Asthan, there are many more gurdwaras which commemorate Guru Nanak's childhood.

**[0:14:45]** We are heading towards Gurdwara Bal Leela, which is made in the memory of Guru Nanak's association with his playmates. It is written in the 'Janamsakhis' that Guru Nanak as a young child was temperamentally peaceful and spiritual.

**[0:15:10]** At a very young age, when the other children would engage in the worldly games, Guru Nanak would engage in spiritual dialogues. For instance, in 'Raag Suhi' (musical measure), he has said, In this profound verse, Guru Nanak expounds on the truth of the worldly game.

**[0:15:34]** He advises not to remain engrossed in sensory pursuits but to acquire knowledge that motivates one to lead a virtuous life. At the age of around seven years, Mehta Kalu decided that Guru Nanak should receive formal all education.

**[0:16:02]** A few yards away, we are now entering the Patti Sahib Gurdwara. The Patti Sahib Gurdwara is built at the site where Guru Nanak received his early education.

**[0:16:29]** Guru Nanak's father enrolled him under the tutorship of Pandit Brij Nath. Under his guidance, young Nanak acquired knowledge of Indic scriptures, Sanskrit language and the Landa script. Later, Mehta Kalu requested Maulvi Qutab-ud-din to teach Persian, the code language of this region. The Maulvi also introduced him to Hijazi, known as Arabic, a language in which Quran and Islamic literature are scripted.

**[0:17:01]** At an early age, Guru Nanak became well acquainted with key insights of the religious texts of two major native religions, Hinduism and Islam. Standing at the site, I can feel Guru Nanak composing the 'Patti Likhi' verse in 'Raag Asa' (musical measure), in which he associates every alphabet with that of spirituality.

**[0:17:29]** For example, he says for the letter 'Sassa', While for the teacher, letter S or 'Sassa' was a mere sound,

**[0:18:50]** Guru Nanak took the opportunity to enrich his teacher, stating that the Creator is the sole One for the entire creation. With simplicity of using letters of the alphabet, the most fundamental building blocks, he explained an important meaning of Oneness. As a part of one Supreme power, We all are equal.

**[0:19:15]** Discrimination is a result of ego. One who understands this truth, is a true scholar. Guru Nanak displayed a disposition to question ritualistic practices. Even during his childhood, he openly challenged social and religious implementations causing a downfall to the basic attributes of humanity.

**[0:19:42]** When Guru Nanak came of age, his parents decided to organize 'Yagnopavit', a religious custom prevalent in Hindu caste system, where only the male child from upper caste families are invested with a sacred thread, called 'Janeu'. Guru Nanak was always a very curious child.

**[0:20:08]** He approached his sister, Baby Nanaki, and asked if she was wearing this thread too? On being told, the thread is supposed to be only for the male members belonging to the upper caste in the caste system, in the caste system, Guru Nanak was very disturbed. He approached the Pandit and asked, what is the purpose of this thread? On not getting a satisfactory answer, Guru Nanak reflected, the caste discrimination and the gender discrimination that existed in the society and he uttered these profound words.

**[0:22:08]** Guru Nanak asked the priest to give him the everlasting sacred thread, made of compassion instead of cotton, woven with a thread of contentment, knotted with modesty and twisted with truth. He emphasized on the importance of human virtues, over outer symbols. There is no thread to restrain

**[0:23:21]** the sensory organs and no thread for lust. He strongly questioned how one can become pious by merely wearing the religious garbs. He opposed religious and social rules, which, based on birth and gender, overlooking actions, virtues and capabilities, deny dignity and respect to fellow human beings. Since childhood, Guru Nanak is known to have cherished the company of spiritualists over worldly activities.

**[0:23:54]** Concerned with Guru Nanak's disinterest in materialistic pursuits, his father assigned him the task of grazing the cattle. This chore became his daily routine. Rai Saleem Bhatti took us to the sites which were once agricultural fields, owned by his ancestor, Rai Bular Bhatti.

**[0:24:23]** The oral tradition says that Guru Nanak would graze his cattle in this area. This place is called Kiara Sahib and here Baba Guru Nanak Dev Ji Maharaj used to graze his cattle. One day, when Guru Nanak was immersed in deep thoughts, his cattle strayed into a crop field.

**[0:24:52]** The farmers seeing Guru Nanak's cattle in his fields, anticipated that they had damaged the crops. in annoyance, The angry farmer complained to the authorities about the carelessness of young Nanak. When the officials inspected the fields, they witnessed no damage to the crops. Emotions control one's actions.

**[0:25:15]** Overpowered by fear, anger and greed, the farmer had made assumed accusations. Sympathizing with the farmers dilemma, who was remorseful of his actions, Guru Nanak calmed him with a metaphoric example of leading a virtuous life. Just as ravished crops can't yield a good harvest,

**[0:27:00]** similarly, we human beings can't reap a fulfilling life, if we lead a path of conflict, jealousy and anger. Rai Saleem Bhatti now took us to the nearby Mall Ji Sahib Gurdwara. I often reflect on some of the events

**[0:27:59]** mentioned in the historical ‘Janamsakhi’ texts, like the shadow of a tree remaining static and a cobra snake spreading its hood to provide shade while young Nanak was asleep. Acceptance or negation of unexplainable phenomena is challenging.

**[0:28:40]** Over time, in the realm of faith, many narratives of inexplicable phenomena get rooted as prestigious miraculous marvels that overshadow philosophical messages. Guru Nanak in his verse, impresses upon the truth that the Creator resides in the entire creation which is one ecosystem.

**[0:29:04]** In my humble understanding, the Creator can wield situations beyond comprehension. Hence, personally, I focus on Guru Nanak's philosophy of Omnipresence over unexplainable marvels. Guru Nanak's parents, concerned about their son's non-attachment to worldly affairs, thought they could alter his personality by getting him married.

**[0:29:38]** When he was 16 years of age, they started looking for a suitable alliance. At this young age, he was betrothed to Ghummi, the only child of Moola Khatri, the father and Chando Rani, the mother. For the marriage ceremony, groom and bride's family gathered at Batala, a city in district Gurdaspur in India.

**[0:30:06]** In present times, due to political reasons, the India-Pakistan border can only be crossed at a few designated points. Hence, we travel from Pakistan to India through the Wagah border and visit Batala. Within the old city of Batala, we visit Gurudwara Dera Sahib, which is built in the memory where Guru Nanak marriage ceremony was held with Ghummi, who was affectionately known as Sulakhni.

**[0:30:53]** This site is believed to have been the home of Moola Khatri. A few lanes away is Kandh Sahib Gurdwara, built at the place where the marriage gathering was hosted. It is believed this wall is probably the remains of the house

**[0:31:46]** where the marriage gathering had stayed. Soon after the wedding, Guru Nanak and Mata Sulakhni traveled back to ‘Rai Bhoe Di Talwandi’, which is today known as Nankana Sahib. The marriage of two individuals is a union anchored in collective growth.

**[0:32:31]** A blissful spiritual relationship is achieved when the ego is renounced and duality is shed. We cross the India-Pakistan border at Wagah and visit Farooqabad, which is at a distance of about 24 kilometers from Nankana Sahib. At the times of Guru Nanak, this place was known as Chuharkhana.

**[0:33:08]** It is at this place that Guru Nanak sowed the seeds of the tradition of ‘Langar’ (free community kitchen). Even at the age of 18 years, Guru Nanak showed no interest about worldly affairs. Concerned about his disposition, Mehta Kalu, his father gave him 20 rupees and requested, do some business out of it.

**[0:33:41]** Guru Nanak, escorted by an attendant, had barely reached Chuharkhana, where they met Bhagat Rein, an ascetic, with his followers, who were hungry. Guru Nanak, being a man of compassion, brimming with the spirit of sharing and service to others, used the entire sum of money to purchase provisions to feed the ascetics.

**[0:34:06]** In this action of selfless service, he demonstrated the spirit of caring for one and all. Food is the basic source to sustain life. When shared and partaken collectively, it evokes feelings of compassion and Oneness. Guru Nanak says, when ignorance is dispelled,

**[0:35:58]** virtues manifest. True transactions are traded in a shop, represented by the human body, a temple for the soul, which needs to be illuminated by igniting knowledge of self-awareness. It is in this state that peace is exuberated. This was a ‘Sacha Sauda’, the true business that he did.

**[0:36:47]** After having spent all the money to feed the hungry ascetics, Guru Nanak returned to Talwandi. Anticipating his father, Mehta Kalu’s displeasure with his decision, he stopped in the outskirts of the city, under a grove of trees, which is now in the premises of the Gurdwara Tambu Sahib. Mehta Kalu was briefed by the attendant about the events at Chuharkhana.

**[0:37:18]** Raging with anger, Mehta Kalu came to Guru Nanak and questioned him for wasting money to feed the ascetics, instead of doing a profitable transaction. Guru Nanak calmly responded that he found no more virtuous and profitable bargain than feeding the hungry. According to ‘Janamsakhi’ accounts, Rai Bular had great affection for Guru Nanak

**[0:38:03]** and held him in high esteem. A noble and pious man, he was inspired by young Nanak’s personality. Guru Nanak, in his early years, started aspiring to experience places of various faiths, which were then famous for pilgrimage congregations.

**[0:38:30]** He expressed this desire to Bhai Mardana, with whom he had a deep bond as this shared similar thought process. Bhai Mardana was a long-time companion of Baba Guru Nanak. He played an integral part in the mission of Baba Guru Nanak to spread the message of humanity. Bhai Mardana was born in this village, now known as Nankana Sahib.

**[0:38:56]** He was born to a 'Mirasi' (genealogy singers) family. At the age of 20 years, Baba Guru Nanak requested Bhai Mardana to accompany him on the spiritual journeys. Mehta Kalu did not give his consent for the travels. Bhai Mardana, also suggested putting the idea on hold as he had the responsibility for his daughter's marriage.

**[0:39:21]** The aspiration to travel indicates that Guru Nanak, from a young age, was inclined to interact with people of diverse belief systems. Concerned with young Nanak's disposition towards philosophical thinking and dispassion for materialistic pursuits, his parents became worried. Assuming that he may be suffering from a disease, Mehta Kalu, his father, called for a 'Vaid', a physician, to examine him.

**[0:39:53]** As the physician held Guru Nanak’s wrist to check the pulse, he withdrew his arm and sang. Guru Nanak was not suffering from any physical ailment.

**[0:40:53]** He was agonized to experience the disease of 'I', the ego in society, which was degenerating the core values of social structure, causing a disconnect amongst fellow beings. To pay our respects to Rai Bular Bhatti, we visited his memorial, which is built on a mound, a short distance from the Janam Asthan Gurdwara in Nankana Sahib.

**[0:41:44]** A true partnership seeks love and strives to gain spiritual wisdom. Guru Nanak says.

## Episode 2: Shafaf Khayal — *Clear Thought*

*[Watch](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=8SBC42GzrI8) · runtime 42:31*

**[0:00:05]** ‘Shafaf Khayal’, ‘Clear Thought’, presents the youthful years of the compassionate defiant who endowed the clarity of unity in diversity. Embodiment of that tradition was Bhai Chand and he imbibed from his elders.

**[0:00:39]** It also explores the legacy of Bhai Mardana, the lifelong friend of Guru Nanak. Guru Nanak establishes that all are equal.

**[0:02:39]** Guru Nanak's elder sister Bebe Nanaki was married to Jai Ram. The couple lived in Sultanpur Lodhi, a town on the eastern bank of the River Bein. The city holds immense importance in the context of the life events of Guru Nanak. In present times, due to political reasons, the India-Pakistan border can only be crossed at a few designated points.

**[0:03:08]** To have insight into the formative years of his adulthood, we visit Sultanpur Lodhi in India through Wagah border. During Guru Nanak's time, this city was under the governance of Daulat Khan Lodhi, a Muslim Governor of the Lodhi dynasty.

**[0:03:35]** Both, Hindu and Muslim communities were then an integral part of the social fabric of this city. Concerned about her brother's future, who was still not showing any interest in worldly affairs, Bebe Nanaki and Jai Ram explored the idea of Guru Nanak relocating to Sultanpur Lodhi. Hoping a change of place may influence Guru Nanak's frame of mind, his parents agreed that he moved to Sultanpur Lodhi.

**[0:04:10]** In Sultanpur Lodhi, we visit the place which is believed to have been the home of Bebe Nanaki and Jai Ram. Jai Ram was a high ranking official, the chief executive of the 'Modi Khana', the State Granary at Sultanpur Lodhi, Jai ram recommended Guru Nanak's name for a job at the ‘Modi Khana’ (State Granary).

**[0:04:40]** Daulat Khan Lodhi appointed Guru Nanak as a 'Modi', a position of immense authority entrusting him with supervising the state granary, keeping daily records of the commodities and maintaining the ledger. Guru Nanak, now having settled with a job in Sultanpur Lodhi, took up a house and got Mata Sulakhni, his wife, to move from Talwandi.

**[0:05:14]** We visit the place where it is believed was once the home of Guru Nanak and Mata Sulakhni in Sultanpur Lodhi. Guru Nanak lived a householders life. Gurdwara Guru Ka Bagh marks Guru Nanak's stay in Sultanpur Lodhi with his family.

**[0:05:41]** Soon they were blessed with two sons, Sri Chand and Lakhmi Chand. Sri Chand, the elder son, chose an ascetic lifestyle, initiating the ‘Udasin’ (ascetic) order, which contributed significantly to spreading the philosophy of Guru Nanak to far and wide lands. Sri Chand passed the leadership of the 'Udasin' (ascetic) tradition to Baba Gurditta, the eldest son of Guru Hargobind, the sixth Sikh Guru.

**[0:06:19]** Lakhmi Chand, the second son of Guru Nanak led the householder's life. At a short distance is the Gurdwara Hatt Sahib, built at the site where the ‘Modi Khana’, the state granary existed. Guru Nanak, now holding a seat of authority at the 'Modi Khana' (State Granary), was exposed to the immoral ways of society.

**[0:06:53]** Corruption was entrenched in the social system. For their benefit, the rich would bribe the authorities. To take advantage, the officials would misuse their power and the ones who suffered were the weaker sections of society. Guru Nanak, an adherent of honesty, sincerely performed his duties.

**[0:07:18]** For the underprivileged, Guru Nanak would distribute an incremental quota of grain. Soon, his reputation grew far and wide. The people at the grassroots were happy with Guru Nanak, and he became popular as a hard working, trustworthy, honest and compassionate officer. A jealous co-worker threatened by Guru Nanak’s popularity complained to the Governor that Guru Nanak was an irresponsible officer who was squandering the state revenue.

**[0:07:53]** Governor Daulat Khan Lodhi ordered a tally of accounts and measurement of the quantity of grains. After a thorough inspection, the appointed officers found the accounts in order and a surplus amount of grains, which some citizens, having noticed Guru Nanak's charitable conduct, had donated for distribution to the needy. Guru Nanak draws attention to the immutable fact that no one can escape consciousness

**[0:09:13]** which has the ledger of one's deeds. As per the law of nature, falsehood perishes and truth prevails. Corruption is the exploitation of entrusted power for personal benefit. Honesty is the fulcrum for a fair democratic society leading to socio-economic equilibrium and harmony. This human rights revolution was seeded by Guru Nanak’s actions at the ‘Modi Khana’ (State Granary).

**[0:09:49]** Gurudwara Hatt Sahib is a testimony of Guru Nanak's practical implementation of practicing what he preached. Honest earning, generous sharing and spiritual contemplation. Bhagirath, the chief of the village Malsihan was a virtuous man. During his visits to deposit the grains as land revenue at the ‘Modi Khana’ (State Granary), he was deeply impressed by the new 'Modi', Guru Nanak, who was proficient in both, his job and knowledge of spirituality.

**[0:10:28]** Bhagirath started visiting Sultanpur frequently to have spiritual interactions with Guru Nanak. The adherents of Guru Nanak started from such first few like Bhagirath. The world of Guru Nanak's goodwill had spread around the region. Deeply touched by Guru Nanak's radical influence in society, Bhai Mardana, who was in Talwandi, desired to meet him.

**[0:10:57]** As chance would have it, Mehta Kalu asked Bhai Mardana to visit Sultanpur and bring back news of Guru Nanak's welfare. At Sultanpur, Bhai Mardana Stayed at Guru Nanak's home. As a routine, in the early hours, Guru Nanak would bathe in the River Bein and then both, he and Bhai Mardana would sing spiritual verses.

**[0:11:31]** One day, Bhai Mardana expressed his financial limitations to have his daughter married. On hearing by Bhai Mardana's dilemma, Guru Nanak requested Bhagirath to facilitate the wedding requirements. Bhagirath visited Lahore to make the arrangements. Here he met Mansukh, a noted trader in the city.

**[0:12:01]** On hearing about the revolutionary thoughts and actions of Guru Nanak, Mansukh joined Bhagirath on his way back to Sultanpur. Mansukh was elated at meeting Guru Nanak and became another one of his early adherents. On having received the goods for the marriage, Bhai Mardana left for Talwandi.

**[0:12:29]** Soon after getting his daughter married, he returned to Sultanpur to be with Guru Nanak. We now visit Gurdwara Ber Sahib on the banks of River Bein.

**[0:12:54]** This gurdwara is a landmark site in the heart of Sultanpur Lodhi City. The quaint scenic beauty of this area was the reason Guru Nanak would meditate here after bathing in the river. How can one attain truth without having to wander physically?

**[0:14:05]** With an abundance of preachers in the realm of organized faiths, I wonder how many have experienced the Truth referred by Guru Nanak. Gurdwara Ber Sahib represents the time Guru Nanak spent on the banks of River Bein, meditating and having discourses with Allah Ditta, a Muslim holy man.

**[0:14:37]** Time and again, saints have been proclaiming that the Creator resides in every aspect of the creation. So can pilgrimage sites provide spiritual solace to the seeker's mind? Guru Nanak message affirms the overarching importance

**[0:15:24]** of the right intentions to attain solace. I wonder, why we continue to endorse ritualism and have not yet evolved to grasp the essence of Guru Nanak's wisdom. To contemplate in seclusion, Guru Nanak would often visit the graveyard on the other side of the river.

**[0:15:53]** Guru Nanak's father-in-law, Moola Khatri, along with Sama Pandit, visited the graveyard to understand, why Guru Nanak was spending much time in isolation. Unable to comprehend the meditative state of mind, Moola Khatri assumed Guru Nanak was possessed and sought assistance from Fakirs to free him from evil spirits.

**[0:16:20]** One morning, when Guru Nanak went to bathe, he did not emerge from the River Bein. Worried by the disappearance, assuming Guru Nanak had drowned, the onlookers raised the alarm. Governor Daulat Khan Lodhi made every possible arrangement for his search, but all efforts went futile. Guru Nanak had probably swum across the river, into the dense forest, to contemplate in solitude.

**[0:16:52]** After three days, he was seen coming out of the River Bein, about three kilometers away from the point he had entered. The Gurdwara Sant Ghat is built to commemorate the emergence of Guru Nanak from the River Bein.

**[0:17:34]** Guru Nanak’s philosophy of Oneness, was pronounced by him at the banks of River Bein, after meditation and contemplation. After this mystic experience, Guru Nanak was endowed with clarity that he wanted to disseminate with one and all. Connected with the Consciousness, Guru Nanak was in awe of the Omnipresent and only saw goodness in the universe.

**[0:18:18]** Guru Nanak, was now in 'Birha', an intense state of dispassion for duality. Seeing Guru Nanak wanting to relinquish his worldly possessions, people believed he had lost his sanity. Guru Nanak stated,

**[0:19:46]** the most honorable religion is that of practicing righteousness. He fearlessly proclaimed that all are equal. On the path of spirituality, there is no space for bigotry and supremacy of any religion, caste, color and gender. This offended the custodians of the prevalent belief systems.

**[0:20:11]** Infuriated, they complained to Daulat Khan Lodhi, the Governor. Guru Nanak was summoned to give an explanation for his statement. Qazi, the arbiter of Islamic law, who supported the complaint, was also present to question Guru Nanak. He asked, if righteousness is the most honorable religion, then what is the importance of faiths, which have been established for centuries by Prophets, Seers, Sages and Bhagats (spiritual beings)?

**[0:20:45]** Guru Nanak replied that a truly religious person is one who follows the true essence of the religion. The Qazi questioned Guru Nanak, since every man of religion follows a path, Muslim or Hindu, so what is Guru Nanak's chosen direction? Guru Nanak replied that he seeks universal kingship and follows the path that leads to Divine Consciousness, which does not see anyone as a stranger and has no religion.

**[0:21:17]** Qazi, discontented with the answer, said, if you are on the path of the Divine, then join us for the 'Namaz'. the Muslim prayer. Guru Nanak agreed and accompanied them to the mosque. We visit the Gurdwara Antaryamta, which is built in the memory of the narrative of Guru Nanak being invited to read the 'Namaz'.

**[0:21:54]** While the Qazi was reciting the 'Namaz', Guru Nanak looked at him and smiled. This infuriated the Qazi, who asked the reason for his amusement. Guru Nanak replied, you were not reciting the 'Namaz' honestly. Even though you are physically present in the mosque, your mind was at home, anxious about the newborn filly. Adorning religious garb and performing rituals is easy, but the real test is practicing the path spiritual leaders subscribe.

**[0:22:28]** A faithful follower of one's faith is the one who is compassionate, humble, reposes complete faith and submits to the Divine Will. Else, the daily prayers, rituals and clothing are nothing but a false show. Even though we aspire to inculcate right actions, what hampers the aspiration?

**[0:23:11]** Illusioned by materialism and tricked by sensory attractions, the mind is subject to delusion. It causes human temperament to oscillate between attachment and detachment, which hampers the desired aspiration to act righteously. After the partition of 1947, due to a population swap based on religion, the city of Sultanpur Lodhi now has a majority Sikh and Hindu population, with the scant presence of Muslims.

**[0:23:50]** This historic mosque within the compound of the old fort is a beautiful representation of inter-faith harmony. Within its premises, the Muslims of the city have kept scriptures containing Guru Nanak's verses as memorabilia of the message of oneness. Finally, Guru Nanak decided to embark on a spiritual journey to visit places of religious, social,

**[0:24:31]** and cultural congregations. He requested Bhai Mardana to accompany him in these spiritual explorations. Both probably felt the need to incorporate music in their journey, and for this, they decided to carry a Rabab, a pluck string instrument, which has the ability to create a percussive sound along with scream vibrations.

**[0:25:08]** Words overlaid with spiritual music help transcend the thoughts of the mind. Before embarking on his journey, Guru Nanak, requested Bhai Mardana to buy a Rabab from an artisan by the name of by Bhai Phiranda. We now travel from Sultanpur Lodhi to Bharoana Village in the basin where the rivulet Bein joins the Beas River.

**[0:25:58]** This gurdwara is dedicated to Bhai Phiranda, an artisan who presented a Rabab to Bhai Mardana. After Bhai Mardana procured the Rabab, Bhai Phiranda accompanied him to personally hand over the instrument to Guru Nanak in Sultanpur Lodhi. In ecstasy, Guru Nanak sang.

**[0:27:41]** Guru Nanak, in his humility, sees himself now prepared to undertake a mission. Having fulfilled his fatherly duty, Bhai Mardana was also ready to be a part of Guru Nanak's mission. Bhai Gurdas, in his writings, mentions.

**[0:28:14]** Historically, Muslim bards who sang ‘Gurbani’, the verses from Guru Granth Sahib (scripture), came to be known as ‘Rababis’. For centuries, they sang spiritual verses in the Gurdwaras. The partition in 1947 divided the subcontinent in two sovereign nations, India and Pakistan.

**[0:28:40]** Unfortunately, this political event not only divided the geography, but also adversely impacted many intertwined cultural aspects, including the ‘Rababi’ (Muslim Bards) tradition. In presents times, due to political reasons, the India-Pakistan border can only be crossed at a few designated points. We now make a detour from the footsteps of Guru Nanak and travel to Lahore in Pakistan through Wagah border to connect with those strands who are keeping the legacy alive.

**[0:29:45]** We visit Mohalla Hussianpura in Lahore where reside the family of the Rababis Mohammad Hussain Lal, Naeem Tahir Lal and Sarfraz Hussain Lal, who claim to be the 18th and 19th generation descendants of Bhai Mardana. This visit has deeply saddened me

**[0:33:37]** with the present conditions of the descendants of the ‘Rababi’ tradition. Their forefathers were soulful preservers of Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana's legacy. To get a glimpse of individuals in Lahore who continue to carry forward the light of Guru Nanak, we visit Dr. Asma Qadri, a professor at the Oriental College in the University of Punjab.

**[0:35:19]** Asma Ji, I am curious to know about your connection with Guru Nanak. Spirituality is beyond the realms of organized belief systems,

**[0:37:31]** best defined as a free flow of personal inquiry into diverse philosophies. Saleema Khawaja, a lawyer by profession, is one such soul who got attracted to Guru Nanak's philosophy by listening to the late Rababi, Bhai Ghulam Muhammad Chand, son of late Bhai Chand, one of the last Rababi’s to perform ‘Keertan’ (singing) at the Darbar Sahib,

**[0:38:17]** the Golden Temple, at Amritsar, in India. I am reminded of a saying by a Japanese scholar,

**[0:40:24]** beholding the wise in reverence will not make one wise. What they sought, does. From Lahore in Pakistan, we cross the India-Pakistan border at Wagah and resume exploring the life journey of Guru Nanak from Sultanpur Lodhi in India In travelling, I reflect on the true meaning of 'sangat', the congregational learning.

**[0:40:52]** It is indeed a practical way in which these few people in Lahore continue to seek beyond the boundaries of faiths.

## Episode 3: Ruhani Rawangi — *Spiritual Departure*

*[Watch](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Q0nvITWhqlc) · runtime 41:01*

**[0:00:05]** ‘Ruhani Rawangi’, ‘Spiritual Departure’, presents the onset of the keen beholder's first odyssey to observe, assimilate and disseminate ‘Tawheed’, Oneness. Guru Nanak intermingles with mystics at the seat of ‘Shakarganj’.

**[0:02:34]** In 1504 AD, at the age of 35 years, Guru Nanak embarks on a journey to awaken the soul of society from the slumber of ignorance. from the slumber of ignorance. Before leaving Sultanpur Lodhi, Guru Nanak visits his sister Bebe Nanaki, requesting her to look out for his family while he is away.

**[0:03:00]** With the concurrence of Bebe Nanaki and his wife, Mata Sulakhni, Guru Nanak, along with Bhai Mardana depart from Sultanpur Lodhi to Saidpur, a city which is now known as Eminabad. We now travel from Sultanpur Lodhi in India to visit Eminabad in Pakistan to explore Guru Nanak's narrative in this city.

**[0:03:51]** At Eminabad, Bhai Lalo, a humble carpenter, welcomed Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana and invited them to stay at his place. During the stay with Bhai Lalo, Guru Nanak would spend time in an isolated area, laden with pebbles, known as 'Rori'. His purpose was to prepare himself, spiritually and physically, for the long and the arduous journeys to be performed in the years to come.

**[0:04:29]** He reduced his diet, spent time in spiritual meditation and slept on pebbles in this place. This Gurdwara is an architectural marvel in which the brickwork has been done in a unique artistic style.

**[0:04:55]** The masons who constructed this building were admirably original and innovative. Gurmukhi characters formed by bricks on the arches convey Guru Nanak's profound verses. Undivided Punjab's largest annual Vaisakhi fair (harvest season) would be held on the grounds of this gurdwara, bustling with people of all faiths.

**[0:05:24]** This scenario changed after the partition of the subcontinent in 1947, which led to the migration of communities. This Gurdwara Rori Sahib in Eminabad was made in the memory of Guru Nanak's stay in the outskirts of the village, before he and Bhai Mardana embarked on their journey.

**[0:05:57]** In 1993, the Pakistan Government resurrected the Rori Sahib Gurdwara and opened it for religious services. We visit Gurdwara Bhai Lalo, which is believed to have been built on the site where Bhai Lalo resided.

**[0:06:31]** This gurdwara was built during the reign of Maharaja Ranjit Singh. Post partition, its premises were used by migrants till the year 1993. In Eminabad, a local feudal lord, Malik Bagho, organized a ‘Brahm Bhoj’, the holy feast for the upper class people. Guru Nanak was also invited but he refused to attend the congregation.

**[0:07:03]** Malik Bhago’s ego was challenged. Infuriated, he asked Guru Nanak the reason for declining the invitation and preferring to be hosted by Bhai Lalo, the low caste carpenter. Guru Nanak replied, the food that he eats at Bhai Lalo's place, tastes like milk because it's been earned through hard work.

**[0:07:32]** But the food that he would eat at Malik Bhago’s place, would taste like blood because it's been earned by violating the rights of the poor people. In this materialistic world,

**[0:08:59]** such people are exceptional who have the wisdom to face the light of knowledge by giving up the darkness of ignorance. This incident brings out the significant precept in Guru Nanak's philosophy about social justice, human rights and integrity. He further expounded that a person is inferior or noble through deeds and not by simply being born in a family of a particular caste or religion.

**[0:10:13]** In the strongest words, Guru Nanak says, depriving anyone of their due right is comparable to eating carcass. Emancipation can only be obtained by truthful living. Immoral acts cannot be justified or nullified by performing religious rituals. Bhai Mardana expressed his wish to visit the shrine of Baba Sheikh Farid Shakarganj at Pakpattan.

**[0:10:45]** From Eminabad, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana travelled through the cities of Makhdoompur Pahoran, Harappa, Burewala, to reach Pakpattan. Proceeding by foot and boat, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana halted at Tulamba, a town close to the banks of the River Ravi. This place is now known as Makhdoopur Pahoran.

**[0:11:14]** We travel to Makhdoompur Pahoran to explore the memories of Guru Nanak's visit to this town. On the trade route from Lahore to Multan, Makhdoompur Pahoran was an important transit hub.

**[0:11:58]** Out here, lived a person by the name of Sajjan who ran a guesthouse. Succumbing under materialistic temptations, he would rob and even kill his guests for their valuables. We visit the erstwhile gurdwara, which is believed to have been built at the site where Sajjan ran his guest house.

**[0:12:27]** Presently, it is operating as the Government Higher Secondary School. The name Sajjan means, the noble one.

**[0:12:54]** Projecting himself as a pious man, Sajjan would dress like a Saint to hide his devious intentions. To attract commuters of all faiths, he had constructed a temple for Hindus and a mosque for the Muslims. To lure travelers he would provide free meals and accommodation. Guru Nanak, however, could see beyond Sajjan’s external appearances.

**[0:13:25]** Guru Nanak would beautifully transform people through the power of his verses. To make Sajjan Thug realize his mistake, he asked Bhai Mardana to play the Rabab and the two sang. A noble person is the one

**[0:14:10]** who takes directions from the inner consciousness. Through the practice of introspection, the mind can be freed from the shackles of worldly desires. Hearing these words, Sajjan Thug realized his misdeeds and began to conduct himself in accordance to the true meaning of his name, Sajjan, a noble one.

**[0:14:33]** This gurdwara was built in the memory of the transformation of Sajjan Thug. Reflecting on the transformation of Sajjan, I wonder, what are misdeeds? Influenced by ego and greed, internal transgressions of moral values disturb the spiritual, emotional and physical equilibrium of oneself and that of the others.

**[0:15:19]** These transgressions are misdeeds. In the outer periphery of this gurdwara, I am sitting in the classroom of a school that was built in the year 1917. Even though the founding community departed from these lands in the year 1947, it is so heartening to see that this school continues to run. The legacy of Guru Nanak to impart knowledge and wisdom continues to thrive.

**[0:16:05]** What a profound saying by Bhagat Kabir. Nothing belongs to me. All that is, is Yours and I submit it to You. Look at the will of the Almighty. The community that made this infrastructure for imparting wisdom and knowledge, has submitted it for the benefit of mankind.

**[0:16:37]** From Makhdoompur Pahoran, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana proceeded to Harappa. We now travel to this historic town, famous for the excavated sites of the Indus Valley civilization, dating back to 2000 B.C.

**[0:17:10]** We visit the erstwhile Nanaksar Gurdwara, which is now being used as the premises for the Government Higher Secondary School. From Tulamba, which is now known as Makhdoompur, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana travelled northwards, along the River Ravi, either by boat or by foot and they reached the town of Harappa.

**[0:17:49]** This gurdwara was made in the memory of their arrival in Harappa. This foundation stone was laid by Sriman Sant Sangat Singh Ji Maharaj of Kamalia village.

**[0:18:19]** After the partition of 1947, which resulted in the migration of communities, this site remained abandoned for years and was later put to good use, to impart education. As I look at the students writing exams, I wonder if today's competitive system, which instills fear, is beneficial for the long term welfare of society?

**[0:19:50]** Irrespective of the situation, one should make sincere efforts and not be influenced by the fear of uncertainty of outcomes. As per local tradition, from Harappa, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana visited Hazrat Baba Haji Sher’s Mausoleum in the outskirts of Burewala.

**[0:20:26]** We are on our way to Burewala, where we will be meeting Baba Rafeeq Dukhi. He has been kind enough to agree to show us the sites of Baba Haji Sher and the gurdwara at Burewala. Hazrat Baba Haji Sher was one of the earliest Muslim Saint in the subcontinent.

**[0:21:40]** He spent his life immersed in mysticism at this village where he lived till 730 AD. At times mystical experiences cause spontaneous ecstatic moments in which experiencers lose control over themselves.

**[0:22:36]** 'Deewana'(insane) in Indic spiritual traditions is akin to insanity, a total loss of control over oneself. For Guru Nanak, the state of insanity is when one fails to recognize the Truth of self. The moment of ecstasy is when one experiences personal transformation.

**[0:23:34]** Baba Rafeeq Dukhi walked us to the gurdwara, which was abandoned in the partition of 1947. This gurdwara, in the close proximity to the shrine of Baba Haji Sher in Burewala

**[0:24:20]** was made in the memory of Guru Nanak's visit. Trailing Guru Nanak's journey, We now travel from Burewala

**[0:26:06]** to Nanak Tibba village in the outskirts of Pakpattan. From Burewala, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana traveled eastwards to Ajodhan, which is now known as Pakpattan. They stayed at this area, which then was a jungle and is now known as the village Nanak Tibba.

**[0:26:35]** Sheikh Kamal, a disciple of Sheikh Ibrahim Farid Sani, was collecting wood in the forest when he heard Guru Nanak sing a hymn while Bhai Mardana played the Rabab. Understanding the essence of the verse

**[0:27:43]** which implied that the entire world is a manifestation of one Omnipresent, Sheikh Kamal rushed to his master, Sheikh Ibrahim, to inform him about his experience of hearing Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana. Sheikh Ibrahim, also known as Sheikh Brahm, was the 12th successor of Baba Sheikh Farid's spiritual seat.

**[0:28:12]** Sheikh Ibrahim warmly welcomed and hosted Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana, who thereafter stayed at Pakpattan for a few days. We now visit the Mausoleum of Baba Farid within the city center of Pakpattan. The city attracts a large number of devotees who visit to pay respect to the revered Chisti Saint.

**[0:28:58]** Baba Sheikh Farid, the 13th century Sufi Saint of the Chisti order, is a towering figure of reverence. The ineffable sweetness of his words, kindness of his actions, warmth of unbiased service and generosity of an ocean, endowed him with the title of Ganj-e-Shakkar, meaning a treasure of sweetness.

**[0:29:26]** At Baba Farid's shrine, we were received by Diwan Shahbuddin Bakhtiyar Chisty. My name is Diwan Shahbuddin Bakhtiyar Chisty. I am from the progeny of Hazrat Baba Farid-ud-Din Masood Ganj-e-Shakkar. I am the 27th generation of the Sufi Saint. The era of Hazrat Baba Faird-ud-Din, starts from 1173 to 1266.

**[0:29:53]** The Sufi philosophy of the Saint is all about Oneness of creation. Exactly. And that is the philosophy of Guru Nanak also because when he writes in his verse in Japji Sahib, he starts with ‘Ik Ongkar’ (Guru Nanak’s doctrine). The numeral one signifies Oneness. And Baba Farid's verses are also enshrined in the 'Guru Granth Sahib' (scripture), and as I am standing at his Mausoleum, I can read in Urdu.

**[0:30:20]** It is written. That is the shrine of Hazrat Baba Farid-ud-Din Masood Ganj-e-Shakkar.

**[0:31:16]** <b>That is the shrine of </b> <b>Hazrat Baba Farid-ud-Din</b> <b>and that is the first ‘Shahzada Nasheen’ (son),</b> <b>Hazrat Badr-ud-Din Suleman.</b> <b>That was his son.</b> True to his demeanour, Baba Sheikh Farid advises,

**[0:32:44]** if you have the power, stay humble. Share, even if you are a pauper. Do not utter even a word of harshness as the Divine resides in all. Food is the intangible manifestation of love.

**[0:33:15]** The practice and institution of free food, 'Langar' was the fundamental constitutional principle of Baba Farid's way of life, which became the hallmark of the culture of Punjab. Even today in Pakpattan, at every nook and corner, one can witness the tradition of 'Langar' (free community kitchen) institutionalized by him.

**[0:33:46]** The tradition of 'Langar' (free community kitchen), the concept of 'Wand Chakna', to shared amongst communities, is common amongst the saints of spiritual disposition. At Pakpattan, at Baba Farid's Darbar (shrine), I am humbled to be having 'Langar' (free community kitchen) amongst everyone here. Baba Farid's other important contribution is to the Punjabi literature.

**[0:34:16]** He used vernacular Punjabi as a language for his spiritual poetry, so the message could reach the masses. This shrine resonates with renditions of 'Qawalas' (Sufi Singers), who for generations have been transmitting the message of Sufi masters to the masses through oral traditions.

**[0:38:40]** For over 200 years, Baba Farid's verses were being transmitted through an oral tradition. When Guru Nanak was staying in Pakpattan, he collated his verses, which later on were enshrined in the 'Guru Granth Sahib' (scripture). The community later built this Gurdwara at the Tibba village in Pakpattan, in the memory of Guru Nanak's visit.

**[0:39:08]** In departing from Pakpattan, what have I gathered from the lives of Baba Farid and Guru Nanak? Many aspire to achieve the realization of Oneness, but only a very few walk the path.

## Episode 4: Paschimi Surya Uday — *Sunrise in the West*

*[Watch](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=e-0Hry-nlks) · runtime 37:15*

**[0:00:04]** ‘Paschimi Surya Uday’, ‘Sunrise in the West’, presents the pragmatic maven's exemplification to awaken the mind from the slumber of ignorance. Guru Nanak evokes rationality in ‘Uttarkaushal’. Learning is the theoretical part of a concept

**[0:02:25]** and understanding the practical component. Guru Nanak’s journeys intertwine both these aspects and provide a repository of valuable insights for effective assimilation of knowledge. Punjab, the land of five rivers in the Indus belt has been a melting pot of diverse cultures, where Central Asian Islamic and Gangetic Hindu cultures intertwined.

**[0:03:05]** Departing from Pakpattan in the Indus region, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana proceeded east towards the Gangetic region. They crossed River Sutlej and travelled through Sirsa, Karah, Pehowa, and Kurukshetra to reach Haridwar. They first visited the city of Sirsa.

**[0:03:33]** In present times, due to political reasons, the India-Pakistan border can only be crossed at a few designated points. Hence, to retrace Guru Nanak's footsteps, we visit India through Wagah border and continue our journey from Sirsa. Located at the edge of the Thar desert, Sirsa is a historic city,

**[0:04:17]** believed to have been established in the third century A.D. by King Saras. We visit Gurdwara Chilla Sahib, made in the memory of Guru Nanak's visit to Sirsa. The oral narrative at this site, which finds no mention in the earliest ‘Janamsakhis’, is that at Sirsa, Guru Nanak visited a local religious fair organized by Fakirs.

**[0:05:13]** His attire made it difficult for people to identify him neither as Muslim nor as Hindu. Confused, when the Fakirs inquired about his religious background, Guru Nanak smiled and said, if he is seen as a human, then disparity based on religion, caste and creed can be dispelled.

**[0:05:52]** Guru Nanak saw the ‘Jyot’, the universal light residing in everyone. Once this knowledge is kindled, it illuminates the internal religion, which sees no difference between you, me and the Creator. From Sirsa, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana travelled to village Karah in the outskirts of Pehowa town.

**[0:06:33]** In Karah village, we visit the historic Gurdwara built by Raja Udai Singh of Kaithal State to commemorate the visit of Guru Nanak. Interestingly, the narrative on the board of this gurdwaras states that in this village, Guru Nanak brought to life a dead camel and made it have a discourse with a local pandit to shatter his ego.

**[0:07:13]** This narrative does not find mention in the earliest ‘Janamsakhis’. One who creates, destroys too.

**[0:07:41]** Similarly, the one in ego, can shed it too, through one's own efforts. I feel through dialogue, Guru Nanak would have revived the divinity of the Pandit. The oral narrative is probably a metaphoric way of conveying a more profound import. The dead camel may be representing Pandit's spiritually dead consciousness, which is recovered through Guru Nanak's words of wisdom.

**[0:08:22]** From Karah, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana travelled to Pehowa, an ancient town from the era of the epic battle of Mahabharata. We now visit the historic Hindu religious site on the edge of 'Sannihit Sarovar', a pond believed to hold sacred waters of the now extinct Saraswati river, which is said to have once existed in this region.

**[0:09:29]** In the Hindu Puranic texts, the city of Pehowa is mentioned as a place of religious significance. People visit Pehowa to bathe in the ‘Sannihit Sarovar’ with the belief that the holy water gives peace to the departed souls, washes the sins of an individual and appeases the Creator.

**[0:10:05]** It is also famous for 'Pind Daan', a ritual for the salvation of the deceased. 'Pind' are ritualistic offerings of balls made of rice flour, wheat flour, dried milk, honey and sesame seeds, which are used by the priest to perform prayers with an expectation of receiving 'Daan', a charitable payment.

**[0:10:44]** Watching innocent people in hope of salvation being influenced and misguided by the priestly class, Guru Nanak sang in antipathy. Spiritual,

**[0:12:10]** social and political affairs influence society. An honorable leader, with positive qualities, blended with divinity, has the ability to uplift the community. However, an astray leader impedes critical thinking, misguides the innocent, and thus causes social and political unrest.

**[0:12:57]** Guru Nanak relentlessly continued to spark the spirit of rational thinking by bringing to notice the shallowness of dogmatic practices and the hypocrisy of leaders. Observing people throng to places designated as religiously important to perform rituals, I wonder, if it is so simple a path to attain redemption.

**[0:13:58]** Only those who eliminate vices within, can achieve divinity. Located on the banks of ‘Sannihit Sarovar’ is Gurdwara Sheesh Mahal, built in the memory of Guru Nanak's visit to Pehowa. And a short distance from ‘Sannihit Sarovar’, we visit Gurdwara Baoli Sahib, which also commemorates the visit of Guru Nanak to Pehowa.

**[0:14:41]** This gurdwara was built by Raja Uday Singh of Kaithal State. We are graced with the aroma of wisdom residing within,

**[0:16:28]** yet, like the musk deer, in futility, we tireless search externally for this inherent fragrance. From Pehowa Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana travelled 30 kilometers east

**[0:17:13]** to the city of Kurukshetra. We now travel to the historic city of Kurukshetra. Kurukshetra, also known as 'Dharam Shetra', is the land where Krishna had given the famous sermon on righteousness, during the War of Mahabharata.

**[0:18:07]** The Guru Nanak arrived at Kurukshetra during the solar eclipse fair, when a large number of people would visit the sacred pond in this city. In accordance with the prevalent belief, an eclipse results in the advent of energy of demonic planets, Rahu and Ketu, the influencers of negativity.

**[0:18:34]** It was believed that one could be saved from their demonic energy by taking a ritualistic bath at ‘Brahm Sarovar’, the sacred pond and by giving charity to the priests at Kurukshetra. On the day of the solar eclipse, when Guru Nanak sat beside the sacred pond observing pilgrims, a family of a chieftain, who had recently lost his province, camped near the sacred tank and started to cook the deer which he had hunted.

**[0:19:10]** Since consumption of meat during a solar eclipse was considered a violation of religious norms, the aroma arising from the cooking pot infuriated the priest, who, along with Nanu Mal, a well acclaimed Pandit, confronted the chieftain. Guru Nanak intervened to stop the squabble and in his unique approach, advised the crowd to use the arrow of right conduct to hunt vices of the fleeting mind and to cook the vices in a pot of contemplation on a fire ignited by burning the ego.

**[0:20:45]** Guru Nanak patiently said that it is futile to fight over the morality of eating meat, as both, meat and vegetation are forms of life. He pointed out that while the custodians of religion condemn meat eating, ironically, they are the ones who sacrifice flesh as ritualistic practice to please the Creator, the life force, which resides in all of creation.

**[0:21:22]** Elaborating on the inequity and double standards in society, Guru Nanak sang. Humans passionately disagree,

**[0:22:25]** forgetting that we all are born of flesh, consummate with flesh, give birth to flesh and are made of the same flesh, in which resides the Omnipresent. Yet, the egocentric mind remains ignorant and commits immorality by discrimination among various forms of people, based on caste, religion, status, color, creed and gender.

**[0:22:59]** In Kurukshetra, we visit the historic gurdwara, which is at a short distance from the ‘Brahm Sarovar’. Gurdwaras Sidh Bati Pathshahi Pehli marks the memory of Guru Nanak’s visit to Kurukshetra.

**[0:23:35]** This gurdwara was built by Maharaja Ranjit Singh in the early 19th century, and in recent times the old structure has been replaced by this new building. In departing from Kurukshetra, I delve into the subject of astrophysics and spirituality, The creation sprang from a singularity, expanded to become observable and will finally contract into singularity.

**[0:24:07]** Doesn't that imply that all of creation and the Creator are One? So why should we, who are part of creation, fear from other aspects of creation or the Creator? Fear hampers our ability to face the challenges of life.

**[0:24:46]** Only the fear of doing wrong is real. All other fears are false. Planets, stars and cosmos being the creation of the Creator, are harmless. It is our wrong actions and thoughts which cause damage. After spending some time at Kurukshetra, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana proceeded eastwards to the city of Haridwar.

**[0:25:20]** We now visit ‘Har-Ki-Pauri’ in Haridwar. The banks of River Ganga at Haridwar are known as ‘Har-Ki-Pauri’, which means, a stairway to God.

**[0:25:53]** For ages, people have been traveling to Haridwar to take a dip in the Holy River Ganga. Located on the banks of River Ganges, the city of Haridwar has been a site for performing the last rites of the deceased.

**[0:26:28]** Guru Nanak reached Haridwar during the festival of Baisakhi, when the town was bustling with pilgrims. At dawn, as the sun rose over the horizon, Guru Nanak observed people perform rituals, chant mantras and also water from river Ganga while facing the sun towards the east. Guru Nanak inquired from a priest about the significance of offering water towards the East.

**[0:27:03]** The priest replied that water is offered to Sun for emancipation of the departed ancestors. On hearing this, the free spirited Guru Nanak turned his back towards the rising sun and started offering water towards the west. Witnessing a ritual being performed contrary to established practices, the onlookers were baffled.

**[0:27:36]** The priest approached Guru Nanak, correcting him that he change his direction towards the rising Sun. To this, Guru Nanak light-heartedly replied that he was offering water to his fields in Punjab, which were in the West. The crowd, which had gathered, was much amused by such an unusual act. The priest smiled and said, it is impossible for the water to reach the fields in Punjab.

**[0:28:07]** Guru Nanak responded, if this is impossible, then how can the ritualistic offerings reach the ancestors who are no longer in this world? This logic was irrefutable. Without confrontation, through a playful act, Guru Nanak had beautifully exemplified the futility of inconsequential rituals.

**[0:28:31]** Demystifying the concept of salvation after death, he clarified that one redeems consequences of one's actions while alive. Guru Nanak's profound message was to seek exaltation

**[0:30:33]** by imbibing and imparting compassion and not through customary practices devoid of honesty. One must strive to take a holy dip in one's consciousness, drawing a line of self-discipline and meditating on universality. At a short distance from ‘Har-Ki-Pauri’, we visit ‘Nanak Vara’, the earliest site built in memory of Guru Nanak's visit to Haridwar.

**[0:31:16]** ‘Nanak Vara’ was built by Bhagat Bhagwan, an ‘Udasin’ ascetic, who was given the service by Guru Har Rai, the seventh Sikh Guru. It is presently maintained by a ‘Dera’ (commune) which traces lineage to Bhagat Bhagwan. We were informed by the locals that Guru Granth Sahib, the Sikh scripture was enshrined at this site up until the year 1937.

**[0:31:48]** In departing from 'Nanak Vara', I reflect on Guru Nanak's emphasis on attaining spirituality through acquiring wisdom. Ironically, this has been slowly replaced with Guru Nanak's imagery. We now visit ‘Guru Nanak-Ann-Khetar’ along the banks of River Ganga, at ‘Har-Ki-Pauri’.

**[0:32:28]** This property is managed by a family who revere Guru Nanak and are carrying on his tradition of ‘Langar’ (community kitchen). This site, built in recent times, has no historic association. We now visit the site of the erstwhile Gurdwara Gyan Godri at 'Har Ki Pauri'.

**[0:33:08]** After the 'Kumbh Mela' stampede in 1966, this gurdwara ceased to exist as the land was acquired by the administration for the development of the entire surrounding area. We now visit Kankhal, a township about ten kilometers away from Haridwar.

**[0:34:07]** Situated near by Sati Ghat, Gurdwara Dera Baba Dargah Singh is dedicated with the visit of Guru Amardas, the third Sikh Guru. Enroute to Kotdwar from Haridwar, Guru Nanak halted at Kankhal.

**[0:34:34]** Later, Guru Amar Das also visited this place. This gurudwara is managed by the 'Nirmala Panchayati Akhara', establish by the grants from the rulers of the Phulkian State of Patiala in Punjab. 'Nirmalas' are a scholarly group within the Sikh faith.

**[0:35:08]** Guru Nanak, in a unique figurative style, conveyed a deep philosophical message about the slumber of ignorance which entraps people in ritualism. Centuries have passed, yet, we remain in a state of deep sleep!

---

<a id="yt-5"></a>
## Episode 5: Tatvagyan — *Essence of Knowledge*

*[Watch](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=p5cE1ELzre0) · runtime 38:14*

**[0:00:06]** ‘Tatvagyan’, ‘Essence of Knowledge’, presents the ambrosial thoughts of the insightful preceptor on the Indic philosophical traditions. Guru Nanak engages in insightful dialogues with the ‘Kanphata Jogis’, the split-eared ascetics at ‘Gorakhmatta’. Open mindedness to discuss and assimilate diverse philosophical views

**[0:02:52]** has historically been a unique strength of the Indic culture. Guru Nanak’s acknowledgment of the six Indic Philosophical Systems Sankhya, Yog, Nyay, Vaisesika, Mimamsa and Vidant is a reflection that he respected freedom of thought.

**[0:03:18]** However, above all, he endorsed the doctrine of Oneness as paramount. From Haridwar, Guru Nanak, and Bhai Mardana travelled to Kotdwar, Srinagar, Kedarnath, Badrinath, Reetha Sahib and reached Nanakmatta.

**[0:03:44]** They first visited the city of Kotdwar. The city of Haridwar is considered as the gateway to the Gods since it is located at the foothills of the Himalayan mountain range, a region which is associated with Puranic (ancient) tales.

**[0:04:19]** Traling Guru Nanak footsteps, we travel to Kotdwar. At Kotdwar, we visit the historic Sidbali temple, located on top of a hill.

**[0:04:51]** On the banks of the River Khoh, Kotdwar is located at the foothills of the Shivaliks. It is the last town before entering the hilly regions of Pauri. During the time of Guru Nanak, this was a place of worship for the ‘Nath Panthi’ sect, followers of the 10th century influential spiritual leader, Guru Gorakhnath.

**[0:05:19]** In those days, this religious sect had a large following across India, Nepal, Pakistan and Afghanistan. They were also known as ‘Jogis’ (ascetics) or ‘Siddhas’ (occults). A few amongst them, who would have their ear lobes split to wear thick earrings would thus be known as Kanphattas (split eared).

**[0:05:49]** Guru Gorakhnath and his predecessor, Guru Matsyendranath lived much before Guru Nanak. However, in the ‘Janamsakhi’ artworks, Guru Nanak is often depicted in dialogue with both of them. These artworks portray Guru Nanak interacting with the successors of ‘Kanphatta Jogi’ (split eared) sect. Having acquired occult powers and observing ascetic discipline, the members of the sect had become egoistic.

**[0:06:26]** Observing their behavior Guru Nanak sang. Acquiring knowledge should result in becoming humble and not egoistic.

**[0:07:05]** In the book ‘Gur Tirath Sangrah’ written in 1884 by Tara Singh Narotam, he mentions, to commemorate Guru Nanak's visit Gurudwara Charanpadaka existed in Kotdwar. The site has now ceased to exist. From Kotdwar, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana travelled to Srinagar on the banks of the Alaknanda River.

**[0:07:44]** We now travel into the Himalayan hills, to the city of Srinagar in Pauri Garhwal. As per Gur Tirath Sangrah by Tara Singh Narotam, The Gurdwara Charanpaduka was built in the memory of Guru Nanak's visit to Srinagar.

**[0:08:14]** It was destroyed in the floods of 1803. Presently, there is no trace of this historical gurdwara in Srinagar. In departing from Srinagar, I reflect on the constant creative process of creation and destruction. In every act of destruction, there is also an act of creation.

**[0:09:01]** The nature of existence involves accepting and integrating the cycle of duality, namely creation and destruction. In destruction resides a new creation and vice-versa. From Srinagar, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana ascended the hills to visit the valley of Kedarnath.

**[0:09:42]** We travel to the high altitude Kedarnath Valley along the Mandakini River. Due to extreme cold weather conditions, Kedarnath Valley is only accessible for six months in a year.

**[0:10:23]** It is a grueling but a picturesque 7 to 8 hours steep climb to reach the valley. Kedarnath is one of the four high altitude pilgrimage sites for Hindus. The other three sites being, Yamunotri, Gangotri and Badrinath. Legend says the 'Pandavas' the five brothers who won the battle of Mahabharata sought forgiveness from Lord Shiva at Kedarnath.

**[0:10:58]** It is for this reason that pilgrims go through severe hardships to visit Kedarnath, to seek forgiveness and to enlighten their souls. Can forgiveness be sought by mere visitation to sites of pilgrimage? It is the intent which results in positive or negative outcomes.

**[0:11:44]** Guru Nanak says, just as one bears the consequences of one's actions, forgiveness, too, can be sought within, through one's internal effort. We are at the Kedarnath Temple, which is situated at the height of 11,775 feet.

**[0:12:25]** The word ‘Kedar’ means the fields. The word ‘Nath’ means the Lord. This valley symbolizes the fields of the Lord. Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana also visited this valley. However, there is no physical memory which signifies their visit.

**[0:12:56]** Kedarnath temple is dedicated to Shiva. Represented by the symbol 'OM', in the Hindu trinity, there are three Gods - Brahma the generator, Vishnu the sustainer and Shiva the destroyer. Guru Nanak, an advocate of oneness, affixed the numeral one before 'Ongkar' (Omnipresent Creator), to represent the One indivisible force which creates, sustains and destroys.

**[0:14:14]** The human mind is veiled with limitations due to which it is unable to view the panoramic vastness of the oneness. It remains myopic due to its divisive outlook and is not able to comprehend the ultimate truth of unity in diversity. From Kedarnath, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana travelled to Badrinath.

**[0:14:48]** We now travelled to Badrinath, a town nestled on the banks of the Alaknanda River in the Himalayas at the height of 10,800 feet. This temple at Badrinath

**[0:15:33]** is one of the holiest pilgrimage sites of the Hindu Vaishnavite belief system. There is a belief that this temple was initially a Jain religious place of worship, which in the eighth century was converted into a Hindu pilgrimage site by Adi Shankaracharya, the South Indian philosopher. Since then the ‘Rawal’ (Head Priest) of the shrine is appointed from ‘Nambudri Brahmin’ (priest) clan from the South Indian State of Kerala.

**[0:16:10]** Guru Nanak says, change is a norm of existence to be gracefully accepted but core human values should be constant, never to be compromised. Evolution is a natural progression of creation.

**[0:17:18]** However, frailty of the human mind is not able to nurture this reality. All forms of devotion are regressive if this quality of nurturing the inner self is not practiced. As per Indic philosophy, 'Tamo', 'Rajo' and 'Sato', are three dimensions spresent in all aspects of creation.

**[0:17:47]** 'Tamo' is inactivity 'Rajo' is activity and 'Sato' is equilibrium. Guru Nanak encourages one to transcend to 'Turiya', the fourth dimension, where duality is shed to experience Oneness. From Badrinath,

**[0:18:35]** Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana travelled through the hilly forests to a scenic terrain, which is today known as Reetha Sahib. Along the hilly routes, we now travel from Badrinath to Reetha Sahib.

**[0:19:27]** village Reetha is nestled within the higher peaks of the Himalayan range in the Champawat region, along the Nepal border. During the 16th century, people of these hills were deeply influenced by Nath Jogis, the ascetics, disciples of Guru Gorakhnath. They had predominating presence in the Indian subcontinent because Guru Gorakhnath was one of the most revered saints of that era.

**[0:20:02]** At the confluence of the Lodhiya and Ratiya rivers, at this tranquil location, existed a commune of ‘Nath Jogis’ (ascetics). living reclusive lives, despite having an in-depth knowledge of spirituality, these ascetics had become temperamentally inhospitable to anybody outside of their commune.

**[0:20:29]** When Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana reached the valley, they were not welcomed by the ‘Nath Jogis’ (ascetics). We visit the ‘Meetha Reetha Gurdwara’ built to commemorate Guru Nanak's visit.

**[0:21:13]** In these foothills of the Himalayas grows a native Sapindus tree, also known as 'Reetha'. The fruit berry of this tree is medicinal. It is also used as a cleanser, known as 'soap berry'. The berry is primarily bitter and inedible but a specific variety of tree bears both, sweet and bitter berries.

**[0:21:44]** Close to the commune, on the banks of the river, resting under one such 'Reetha' tree which bore, both, sweet and bitter berries, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana sang. Akin to the ‘Reetha’ tree

**[0:23:41]** bearing both, sweet and bitter berries, and experiance the hostility of the knowledgeable ascetic commune, Guru Nanak explained that every aspect of creation has both, negatives and positives, bitterness and sweetness, which are designed by the Creator to facilitate learning to cleanse one's thoughts and actions.

**[0:24:10]** In humility, one should embrace others without animosity or ego and aspire to learn from situations. Guru Nanak conveyed to the ascetics that knowledge bears fruits. It multiplies when shared but diminishes when hoarded. On hearing the profound message of Guru Nanak, the ‘Nath Jogis’ welcomed him and Bhai Mardana.

**[0:24:41]** Both spend a few days in this area to interact with the members of the commune. According to locals, for years, the lineage of the ‘Nath’ commune continued the association with Guru Nanak by enshrining the 'Guru Granth Sahib' the Sikh scripture in their ‘Muth’ (Temple).

**[0:25:17]** This tradition was prevalent till the ‘Meetha Reetha Gurdwara’ was constructed next to the ‘Muth’ (Temple) in 1960s. Adjacent to the ‘Meetha Reetha Gurdwara’, we visit the ‘Muth’ (Temple) dedicated to Guru Gorakhnath. Experiencing the sweet and bitter Reetha fruits at this site,

**[0:26:29]** I introspect on my sweet and bitter attributes and what I learn from them. Splendor is present in every aspect of Creation, also within us.

**[0:26:59]** The value of a situation depends on how it is reckoned. When appreciated, it becomes positive like the beloved. When discarded, it works against us like a foe. Descending from the hills of Reetha Sahib, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana reached the town of Gorakhmatta on the banks of the Deuha River in the plains of Terai.

**[0:27:32]** This town is now known as Nanakmatta. We now travel to Nanakmatta, making the first stop at Nanak Sagar Reservoir. Situated on the banks of the Deuha River,

**[0:28:20]** this town was once known as Gorakhmatta, or Sidhmatta. In reverence of Guru Nanak's visit to this place, it is now known as Nanakmatta. The reservoir on the Deuha River is now known as Nanak Sagar. To commemorate Guru Nanak's visit, the government has constructed a memorial hill in the form of a well within the Nanak Sagar Reservoir.

**[0:29:08]** We now visit Gurudwara Nanakmatta Sahib, which is the main site commemorating Guru Nanak's visit. Admirers and adherents of Guru Nanak have been gathering at this site for centuries.

**[0:29:40]** This is evident from the writings of ‘Khulasat-ut-Twarikh’, a Persian account written in 1695, which states "Nanakmatta is a place where disciples and devotees of Nanak gather in large numbers to pay obeisance and offerings." Before 1937, this gurdwara was maintained by 'Mahants' (Chief Priests) and thereafter its management was taken over by a local committee.

**[0:30:25]** This gurdwara is a great manifestation of inter-faith harmony as its premises were expanded by a ground grant by a man belonging to the Muslim faith, named Nawab Mehndi Ali Khan. During the time Guru Nanak visited Gorakhmatta, it had a thriving settlement of ‘Nath Jogis’ (ascetics), the followers of Guru Gorakhnath.

**[0:30:55]** Over time, the community of ‘Nath’ ascetics have moved from this region. In the memory of Guru Nanak's visit. the town is now known as Nanakmatta. At this place, Guru Nanak and the ‘Nath Jogis’ (ascetics) had an exchange of spiritual thoughts. The ‘Jogis’ (ascetics) enquired about Guru Nanak's faith and the name and credentials of the teacher who initiated him.

**[0:31:29]** Guru Nanak replied, humanity is my faith and I learn from the infallible Omnipresent. He says, what scale,

**[0:33:24]** what weights and which assessor should I call upon? Which mentor should I look up to and from whom should I ask the worth, as no one knows the limits of the all pervading beloved teacher? Only with thoughts has the balance scale, self-awareness, as the measuring weight, and through selfless service, I can be a beneficiary.

**[0:33:57]** I have no scale with which to measure the infinite magnitude of the Omnipresent. Fascinated by Guru Nanak's gripping response and assuming he would be an aspirant to join their commune, the ‘Jogis’ (ascetics) invited him to adopt their yogic attire. Guru Nanak calmly replied. Salvation is not attained by adorning outward garb.

**[0:36:00]** It requires treating the entire existence as one and remaining unaffected by attractions, while performing worldly duties. Guru Nanak's message to the ‘Jogis’ (ascetics) was to nurture the death of desires and sustain a virtuous existence. I continue to struggle to achieve internal discipline, often reminding myself that there is indeed a way to attaining equipoise.
