## Episode 20: Rangeen Guldasta — *Colourful Bouquet*

*[Watch](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=CpJOiAgIW78) · runtime 61:33*

**[0:00:06]** ‘Rangeen Guldasta’, ‘Colourful Bouquet’, presents a vibrant culture that emits Guru Nanak’s fragrance. Along the banks of ‘Sindhu’, Guru Nanak, the all embracing protagonist infuses colours of harmony. After spending time with the families at Nankana Sahib

**[0:03:01]** and Sultanpur Lodhi, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana embarked on the third odyssey, estimated to have commenced in 1520 A.D. This journey took them towards the west to visit important places of religious congregation for the Muslims. From Sultanpur Lodhi, they travelled to Pakpattan, Multan Uch, Sukkur, Shikarpur, and Mirpur Khas.

**[0:03:35]** Travelling further south they visited Lakhpat, Koteshvar and from here sailed to Somiani to visit Hinglaj. From Sultanpur Lodhi, they sailed on the Sutlej River to the city of Pakpattan. In tracing Guru Nanak’s footsteps, we cross the India-Pakistan border at Wagah and resume the journey from Pakpattan.

**[0:04:34]** Guru Nanak was visiting Pakpattan for the second time to interact with Sheikh Ibrahim Farid Sani, also known as Sheikh Brahm, the 12th successor of Baba Sheikh Farid's spiritual seat. Guru Nanak and Sheikh Ibrahim exchanged spiritual thoughts

**[0:05:19]** through the medium of verses. In one such occasion, Sheikh Ibrahim sang Baba Farid's verse to express the importance of simple living and high thinking. Thereafter, Guru Nanak

**[0:06:05]** sang a verse endorsing Baba Farid’s philosophy of unreserved love, a feeling which evokes the clarity of unity in diversity. In response, Sheikh Ibrahim expressed

**[0:07:02]** that even while leading a life of simplicity, the frivolous mind deviates from the path of unison. In reply, the Guru Nanak sang. Guru Nanak says, when the mind recognizes the truth,

**[0:08:09]** it gains the strength akin to a sharp metaled knife that can slaughter avariciousness. During this trip,

**[0:08:58]** with the help of Sheikh Ibrahim, Guru Nanak collated more verses of Baba Farid. This intensive collation was later inscribed in the ‘Guru Granth Sahib’, the Sikh scripture. It is believed during his stay at Pakpattan, Guru Nanak composed the first nine stanzas of the ‘Asa-Ki-Var’ a composition contained in the Guru Granth Sahib.

**[0:09:27]** From Pakpattan, Guru Nanak and and Bhai Mardana travelled on foot to the city of Multan. In tracing Guru Nanak's footsteps, We travel from Pakpattan to Multan. Located on the banks of the river Chenab, the town of Multan in the state of Punjab dates back to antiquity.

**[0:10:08]** Prehistoric texts mention that the legendry tale of Prahlad and Hiranyakashipu took place in this town. In the year 326BC, when Alexander, the Greek King, invaded Multan, it was under the rule of the Mali dynasty. Hiuen Tsiang, the seventh century Chinese traveller has also written about Multan’s prominence.

**[0:10:35]** In the 8th century, Islam was introduced to Multan through the traders and invaders from Central Asia. During Guru Nanak’s visit, Multan was an important center for Muslim Fakirs of different traditions. Multan is a city of Saints.

**[0:11:00]** It is close to the place where the five rivers of the Punjab merge with the mighty River Indus. It is probably for this reason that the Sufi Saints believe that in Mulatn, one can lose one's ego, akin, to the loss of the individual identity of the five rivers of the Punjab. We visit the mausoleum of Sufi mystic, Sheikh Baha-ud-din Zakariya.

**[0:11:42]** Sheikh Baha-ud-din Zakariya was a 12th century spiritual leader who established the Surhawardiya order of Baghdad in this region. It is believed that he was from the lineage of Prophet Muhammad. During the journey through the south of Punjab, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana visited the shrines of three of four Sufi friends, Sheikh Baha-ud-din Zakariya, Sheikh Jalaluddin Bukhari and Sheikh Farid Ganj-e-Shakkar.

**[0:12:19]** These Sufi saints were the pioneers of the 13th century Chisti and Suhrawardiya orders. Famously known as the ‘Char Yaars’ their shrines are important places of Islamic religious congregation, probably a reason why Guru Nanak visited them. This profound verse about the omnipresence of God was recited by Guru Nanak at this mausoleum of Sheikh Baha-ud-din Zakariya.

**[0:13:00]** When Guru Nanak arrived in Multan, the then ‘Makhdum’ of this mausoleum warmly welcomed him and the two spent days in spiritual dialogues. ‘Makhdum’ is an Arabic word which means a teacher.

**[0:13:26]** In the subcontinent, it is a title bestowed upon the spiritual head of a Sufi shrine. Makhdum Bahavadi at the mausoleum of Sheikh Baha-ud-din Zakariya mentioned that he is aware that Guru Nanak saw no difference between a Hindu and a Muslim. However, he inquired if the Supreme Power resides in both, Hindus and Muslims.

**[0:13:52]** In reply, Guru Nanak sang. Guru Nanak says,

**[0:15:17]** indeed the One Supreme Power resides in all. It is the Creator who permeates and beholds one and all. The ancient Prahladpuri Temple

**[0:16:11]** made in the reverence of Bhagat Prahlad is located adjacent to the shrine of Sheikh Baha-ud-din Zakariya. In present times, this site is in ruins because its caretakers, the Hindu community of Multan, migrated to newer lands after the religious divide during the partition of 1947. Bhagat Prahlad in ‘Gurbani’ (Guru Granth Sahib) is referred as a ‘Gurmukh’, the one whose face is towards righteousness.

**[0:16:44]** This ancient temple in Multan is a testimony to the ‘Puranic’ (ancient) tale of Bhagat Prahlad. ‘Bhagavat Puran’, the prehistoric scriptural literature has a compilation of moral stories. In it, one of the tales is of Bhagat Prahlad and his father King Hiranyakaship, whose name means, the beholder of materialism, representing ego.

**[0:17:13]** The name Prahlad means unwavering positivity, which represents the strength to defy negativity. King Hiranyakaship had gained a boon which protected him from being killed by any human or animal, during day or night, indoors or outdoors, or by any weapon.

**[0:17:40]** This grace made Hiranyakaship arrogant and he demanded to be worshiped. Bhagat Prahlad, nurtured by the values of his mother, Kayadhu, declined his father's demand. This infuriated King Hiranyakaship, who connived with his sister Holika, who had a boon to remain unscathed by fire.

**[0:18:05]** When Holika walked into a blazing pyre with Prahlad, she perished, while Prahlad remained unscathed. Thereafter, defying all parameters of the boon, Narasimha, a half human, half animal killed King Hiranyakaship. To commemorate this triumph of righteousness over oppression, Hindus celebrate Holi, the festival of colours.

**[0:18:41]** Even though Guru Nanak and Bhagat Prahlad lived centuries apart in the ‘Janamsakhi’ artworks, the two are depicted in dialogue. In my humble opinion, these artworks convey the philosophical alliance between Guru Nanak and Bhagat Rahlad. Guru Nanak mentions the essence of Prahlad's devotion in the verse.

**[0:19:54]** Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana travelled further south from Multan on the River Chenab to visit Uch. Tracing Guru Nanak’s footsteps, we travel from Multan to Uch. In the year 325 B.C., King Alexander,

**[0:20:38]** the Greek ruler, founded a city called Alexandria, which is believed to be the erstwhile name of the city of Uch. After the conquest of this region by Central Asian invaders in the seventh century, Uch attracted many Sufi saints, hence it is known as Uch Sharif, the exalted city.

**[0:21:06]** The town of Uch is in the southernmost tip of the Punjab. In the 12th century, it was known as Deogarh, which means a stronghold of God. Today, this town is famous for the historic shrines of Sufi saints. In Uch, we visit the mausoleum of the 12th century Sufi Saint, Syed Jalaluddin Bukhari.

**[0:21:39]** A native of Bukhara in Uzbekistan, he was an Islamic missionary who was known as Surkh Posh for adorning red attire. He was popular amongst people of all strata, gender and beliefs. It is said that one of his renowned female disciples was Lal Ded, who broke social biases of gender and religious discrimination.

**[0:22:09]** Lal Ded embraced the overlapping philosophies of Sufism, Islam, Shaivism and Buddhism. In the region of Kashmir, Lal Ded is considered a patron saint of both, Hindus and Muslims. In the 13th century, the famous Sufi Saint, Jalaluddin Bukhari from Bukhara moved to Uch and settled in this town.

**[0:22:42]** This shrine in Uch Sharif is where he lies buried. When Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana visited Uch, the then ‘Makhdum’ of this shrine, by the name of Haji Sheikh Abdul Bukhari received them. The musty room containing Syed Jalaluddin Bukhari’s coffin has captivating interiors.

**[0:23:07]** Multiple wooden pillars holding the flat roof are carved with intricate designs. Walls are decorated with ornate glazed ties, and the ceiling with hand-painted floral and geometric designs cast in lacquer. The room is filled with the graves of others saints and relatives of the Sufi Saint, Jalaluddin Bukhari.

**[0:23:32]** It seems as if they are all still holding a congregation. Many Sikh authors have reported that relics of Guru Nanak are present with the ‘Makhdums’ of Uch. We visit the home of Syed Zamurd Hussain Hasnie Al Hussaini, the present ‘Makhdum’, the caretaker of the shrine of Sayed Jalaluddin Bukhari.

**[0:24:47]** On a closer look at the scripture claimed as a gift by Guru Nanak to the ‘Makhdums’, we found it to be a printed version of the ‘Sukhmani Sahib’ verses of Guru Arjan Dev, the Fifth Sikh Guru. The other relics referred by the caretakers were not presented. Even though these relics are not of antiquity, they are a medium through which the lineage of the ‘Makhdums’ of Uch associate themselves with Guru Nanak.

**[0:25:38]** Only if true to oneself, can one be true to others. Guru Nanak reminds us that Truth is high but living a life of truthful conduct is supreme. While Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana were being hosted by Makhdum Haji Abdul Sheikh Bukhari, Makhdum Bahavadi of Sheikh Baha-ud-din Zakariya’s shrine of Multan, also arrived in Uch.

**[0:26:07]** The two ‘Makhdums’ decided to visit Mecca for performing ‘Haj’. Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana accompanied them on their boat from Uch. Sailing through the Rivers Chenab and Indus, they reached Sukkur in Sindh. In Guru Nanak’s footsteps, we travel from Uch to Sukkur in Sindh.

**[0:27:00]** Sindh is in the crossroads of land and sea routes. It borders Punjab and Balochistan in Pakistan and Rajasthan and Gujarat in India. Historically, cultures, civilizations, faiths and philosophies converged in Sindh. It is probably for this reason that Guru Nanak also visited Sindh.

**[0:27:25]** to interact with different cultures and faiths. Famous for the Mohenjo-daro civilization, Sindh derives its name because of its close proximity to the Sindhu River, which is also known as the Indus. For centuries, Hinduism and Buddhism were the integral belief systems of this region.

**[0:27:59]** Later, Sufism, too, blossomed here. Historically, natives of Sindh have been known for the syncretism. Guru Nanak's message of universal fellowship and monism became deeply embedded in the socio-religious beliefs of Sindh. The philosophy of Guru Nanak resonated with the people of this region, primarily of Hindu background, who came to be known as ‘Nanakpanthis’.

**[0:28:37]** At Sukkur, we visit the island in the River Indus, where exists a historic ‘Nanakpanthi’ temple, known as Sadhu Bela. Sindh is a mystical land which has always fascinated me. It is here that the faiths start merging.

**[0:29:02]** Whether one is a Hindu, Sufi or a Sikh, they become one. At Sadhu Bela Temple, inscriptions of Guru Nanak's verses on its walls are a testimony of the impact of Guru Nanak's philosophy in this region.

**[0:29:50]** In the sanctum sanctorum of the temple, both, ‘Bhagvat Gita’, the Hindu scripture, and the Guru Granth Sahib, the Sikh scripture are enshrined. The walls are adorned with pictures of Sikh Gurus and Hindu deities. The temple caretaker warmly received us and read a few verses from the Guru Granth Sahib, the Sikh scripture.

**[0:30:39]** Sadhu Bela temple gives an insight into the rich ethos of the Sindhi community. In the memory of Guru Nanak's travel through Sukkur in Sindh, ‘Nanakpanthi Udasin Sadhus’ establish this site of Sadhu Bela in the center of the Indus River. Bankhandi Sadhu, a ‘Nanakpanthi’ from Nepal, moved to Sukkur in the year 1823 to spread Guru Nanak's message of Monism.

**[0:31:13]** An invocation to Bankhandi Sadhu is inscribed on a marble slab, along with a verse of Guru Nanak from the Guru Granth Sahib, the Sikh scripture. Interestingly, we observed that the historical inscriptions have been tampered.

**[0:31:46]** Guru Nanak, which was originally engraved on this marble plaque is painted over as ‘Guru Sri Chand’, the name of Guru Nanak’s son who is associated with the ‘Udasin’ (ascetic) tradition. This overwriting is one of the many indicators of the socio-political forces which fragmented the diversity of the culture of ‘Nanakpanthis’, the bouquet of believers of Guru Nanak.

**[0:32:27]** The Sadhu Bela Temple gives an insight to the erstwhile rich synergic Sindhi community. Unfortunately, the ‘Nanakpanthi’ culture of Sindh was fragmented in the partition of 1947 based on religion. Insecurities overpower rational thinking creating distrust and disharmony amongst communities.

**[0:33:10]** Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana accompanied the Makhdums of Multan and Uch and travelled on foot from Sukkur to Shikarpur. In tracing Guru Nanak’s footsteps, we travel from Sukkur to Shikarpur. At Shikarpur, we visit Dada Rewa Chand’s residence to experience his love for Guru Nanak.

**[0:35:38]** We visit the Shankar Anand Bharati Darbar which is one of the oldest sites established in the memory of Guru Nanak's visit to Shikarpur. Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana spent a few days in Shikarpur.

**[0:36:32]** Their stay on the banks of a river is depicted in this painting at the Shankar Anand Bharati Temple in Shikarpur. This temple is a syncretic place of worship for the ‘Nanakpanthi’ communities of this region. From Shikarpur, Guru Nanak and and Bhai Mardana headed back towards River Indus.

**[0:37:11]** They boarded a boat and sailed southwards. The Makhdum of Uch and the Makhdum of Multan took the land route towards Mecca. From Shikarpur, we make a detour from Guru Nanak's footsteps to visit Sehwan Sharif to explore memories of Bhagat Sadhna, a saint whose philosophy was aligned with that of Guru Nanak's.

**[0:38:13]** Bhagat Sadhna, also known as Sadhna Qasai, was a saint born in 1180 A.D. to a Muslim butcher family in Sehwan Sharif, a city in Sindh province of Pakistan. Much after the time of Bhagat Sadhna's birth, the city became famous for the shrine of Lala Shabaz Qalandar. The Sufi ‘Malangs’, the carefree mystics,

**[0:39:06]** and ‘Qalandars’, the saints who associate themselves with boundary less existence, associate themselves with the shrine. Bhagat Sadhna was also of the same spiritual disposition. It is said, Bhagat Sadhna was presented a ‘Saligram’, aniconic stone representing the Hindu deity Vishnu by a Brahmin.

**[0:39:32]** In paying respect, Bhagat Sadhna would first worship the stone and then use it for weighing meat for his customers. This action angered the Hindu Brahmins and the Muslims rebuked him for worshiping an idol. The authorities viewed this as disrespect of religions and sentenced him to being bricked alive in a wall.

**[0:39:56]** It is said that the wall fell and Bhagat Sadhna survived. In my humble opinion, the incident of the wall breaking is a metaphor for Bhagat Sadhna’s disassociation from dogmatic norms prescribed by religious authorities. Bhagat Sadhna spent most of his adulthood in the city of Sirhind in East Punjab in India where he breathed his last.

**[0:40:33]** Even though Bhagat Sadhna and Guru Nanak were born centuries apart, yet their core philosophies intertwined. One of Bhagat Sadhna’s verse is inscribed in the Guru Granth Sahib, the Sikh Scripture. From Shikarpur,

**[0:41:54]** Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana sailed on the River Indus and visited Mirpur Khas. In tracing Guru Nanak’s footsteps, we travel from Shikarpur to Mirpur Khas. While traveling through Sindh, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana stopped at the city of Mirpur Khas.

**[0:42:38]** This gurudwara in the center of the city was built in their memory. This gurudwara was abandoned during the partition of the Indian subcontinent in the year 1947, when the followers of Guru Nanak had to migrate from Mirpur Khas.

**[0:43:15]** From Mirpur Khas, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana continued their journey by boat on the River Indus. Traveling through what was then called the River Kori, which merged into the Arabian Sea, they reached the port of Lakhpat. From Mirpur Khas, we travel back to Punjab in Pakistan to cross the Pakistan-India border and resume trailing Guru Nanak’s footsteps from Lakhpat in the Indian state of Gujarat, Kot Lakhpat was once a prosperous town.

**[0:44:03]** A fertile agricultural area, important for trade and pilgrimage. It hosted people of diverse belief systems. The fate of Lakhpat was decided by the 1819 Rann of Kachchh earthquake. The ground sank by 15 feet and filled the creeks with saline seawater, turning the once fertile region into barren land.

**[0:44:41]** The River Indus and Kori changed their course away from the city of Lakhpat. Thus, the once prosperous port town lost its importance. After the partition of 1947, this region has been transformed into a high security border zone. When Guru Nanak travelled to Kot Lakhpat, this town was known as Basta Bandar.

**[0:45:19]** This shrine was built by the ‘Udasins’ (ascetic) and is now managed as a gurdwara. Land records indicate that this site was built in the early 19th century. The native ruler, in reverence of Guru Nanak and the ‘Nanakpanthi’ followers, had allotted a nearby village named Koriyani to this site to generate revenue for its maintenance.

**[0:45:57]** From Lakhpat, we make a detour to visit Koriyani village at a distance of around 20 kilometers. At Koriyani, the villages guided us to a dilapidated building next to a lake.

**[0:46:28]** They mentioned, this site was built by the ‘Udasins’ (ascetics) and was managed by the same management who had built the site in Lakhpat in the memory of Guru Nanak. This building, which is now in ruins, was abandoned in the 1960s.

**[0:47:26]** In departing from this Indo-Pak border region of Lakhpat and Koriyani, I reflect, even though human beings share certain characteristics with other species, the uniqueness is in their cognitive ability and co-operative flexibility. Yet, they falter and create divisions. Guru Nanak compares different states of human mind with the characteristics of other species, prompting us to emulate the positive virtues and discard the negatives.

**[0:48:29]** From Lakhpat, Guru Nanak and by Bhai Mardana walked along the coastline and reached Koteshvar. In Guru Nanak's footsteps, we travel from Lakhpat to Koteshvar. In the Kachchh district of Gujarat, Koteshvar is located at the mouth of the Kori Creek and is famous for the ancient Koteshvar Temple.

**[0:49:20]** Historically, ‘Shaivites’, the followers of Lord Shiva would visit both, the Koteshvar and Hinglaj temples as part of their pilgrimage. During Guru Nanak's visit the ‘Kanphatta Jogis’ (split eared ascetics), the followers of Guru Gorakhnath, were the primary caretakers of this site.

**[0:49:45]** It is believed that Guru Nanak had dialogue with them at Koteshvar. Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana boarded a boat at Koteshvar and travelled on the Arabian Sea to the port of Somiani. We now travel from India to Pakistan and resume tracing Guru Nanak's footsteps from Somiani in Balochistan.

**[0:50:39]** Somiani is a coastal town northwest of Karachi. It was once a prosperous natural port, a hub for trade with Central Asia. Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana travelled on land from Somiani, and passing through Chandrakup, they reached Hinglaj. In Guru Nanak's footsteps, traveling from Somiani to Hinglaj, we visit Chandrakup, the cluster of Hingol mud volcanoes.

**[0:51:21]** The word ‘Chandra’ means Moon and ‘Kup’ means a mud basin. One of the mud volcanoes, which is around 300 feet high, is a site of pilgrimage for the Hindus who visit the Hinglaj Temple. These mud basins which are formed by volcanic activities are considered as physical representation of Lord Shiva's anger.

**[0:51:52]** From Chandrakup, we proceed further to the Hinglaj Temple. Hinglaj is situated on the West Bank of the Aghor River in Balochistan, close to one of the mountains of the Makran coastal range. The shrine of Hinglaj Temple is in a small natural cave

**[0:52:44]** in the hilly area of the Lyari Tehsil. Known as one of the 51 sacred ‘Shakti Peeths’, it is dedicated to Shiva’s consort Sati. It is believed, Sati's severed head fell at this place. In the Hindu tradition, Hinglaj is an important place of worship for ‘Shaktism’, feminine worship.

**[0:53:08]** A stone, which is worshiped as Hinglaj Mata, is known as ‘Nani Ka Mandir’ by Hindus and ‘Nani Ka Haj’ by Muslims. During Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana’s visit to Hinglaj, the ascetics of the ‘Nathpanthi’ sect, had a strong presence in this region.

**[0:53:35]** Looking at Guru Nanak's unique attire, they were unable to discern if he was a ‘Haji’, a Muslim pilgrim on his way to perform 'Haj', the Islamic pilgrimage, or if he was a Hindu pilgrim visiting ‘Nani Ka Mandir’. When they inquired about his faith, Guru Nanak sang.

**[0:55:08]** Guru Nanak discounts religious and ritualistic constructs as rules of conduct prescribed by society. He says, those who give precedence to universal love are beings of a true religion. They are beyond the realm of concepts such as pilgrimages, identities, fasting, and garb.

**[0:55:35]** In this dry and barren region of Balochistan, along the River Aghor, in the hills of Hinglaj, there is sight made in the memory of Guru Nanak's visit, which we are going to go and explore. These barren rocky mountains are daunting, their magnificence challenged our spirits and the scorching heat tested our resilience.

**[0:56:01]** Unsure of our physical capabilities, with conviction, we started the climb. The initial ascent was difficult, the latter part was exhausting, and the final destination was gratifying. I guess this process of scaling the mountain is synonymous to surmounting our daily challenges. The ‘Nanakpanthi’ and the Hindu community

**[0:57:34]** of Balochistan and the Sindh region have made a memorial with a foot impression at this site, which represents the visit of Guru Nanak in the region of Hinglaj. Close to the site, there is another memorial with impressions of the foot of Guru Gorakhnath made by people of faith.

**[0:58:05]** The memorial in the form of Guru Nanak's foot impression is an endorsement of love and reverence of the local Hindu community for Guru Nanak. For me, the foot impression is a reminder for us to walk on the path of Oneness, for which Guru Nanak had travelled to such remote and harsh terrains. British author, A.W Hughes, in his book entitled, 'The Country of Balochistan', published in the year 1877, has mentioned, the ‘Gitchki’ tribe.

**[0:58:48]** He has classified them as Sikhs, the believers of Guru Nanak, who resided in the Hinglaj, Makran and Kalat regions. After the partition of 1947, There are no ‘Gitchki’ Sikhs residing in these regions. After having spent some time with the mystics at Hinglaj, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana travelled back to Somiani and took the sea route to the Persian Gulf.

**[0:59:33]** Water that emerges from the ocean, momentarily creates an identity as a wave but soon loses its singularity and merges with the ocean. This phenomenon reminds me of Guru Nanak's verse, which explains the fallacy of individuality, which gets dissolved when 'Atma', the awareness, becomes one with ‘Paratama’, the wisdom.

<a id="yt-21"></a>
## Episode 21: Wahdat-al-Wajud — *Unity of Existence*

*[Watch](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=LbzSbaxNSTw) · runtime 49:56*

**[0:00:03]** ‘Wahdat-al-Wajud’, ‘Unity of Existence’, presents the resolute harmonizer's conviction in wholeness. In the cradle of civilizations, Guru Nanak states that awareness of Omnipresence is an oasis that can quench the soul.

**[0:00:51]** There is a lot of ‘Gurbani’ (verses) in ‘Guru Granth Sahib’ in the Persian language, which proves that he definitely did come to Iran. One can feel Guru Nanak’s profound humility

**[0:03:01]** through expressions in his verses for he understates himself and glorifies the attributes of the Creator and its creation. On the contrary, I have a tendency of overrating my personality and noticing demerits in others. From Somiani, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana travelled to the ancient port of Al-Aswat, Mecca, Medina, Najaf, Karbala, Baghdad, Tabriz, Tehran and reached Mashhad.

**[0:03:43]** From Somiani, they sailed to Al-Aswat, and then proceeded to Mecca. In tracing Guru Nanak’s footsteps, we travel from Pakistan to Saudi Arabia. Saudi Arabia hosts the holiest Islamic cities of

**[0:04:45]** Mecca and Medina. With the birth of Prophet Muhammad, Islam emerged in these lands in the seventh century. The modern day Kingdom of Saudi Arabia, founded in the year 1932, comprises of the two historical desert regions of Hejaz and Nejd.

**[0:05:12]** Saudi Arabia is the largest country in the Arabian Peninsula. Having travelled for days on rough seas, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana landed at the Port of Al-Aswat in Saudi Arabia. From here, they took the land route, crossing harsh and hot sand dunes and reached the city of Mecca.

**[0:05:51]** The word ‘Mecca’ is believed to be a synonym for ‘Makkah’ which means, mother of all settlements. Located in a desert valley, this city was a commercial hub much before it became the holiest place of pilgrimage for the Muslims in the seventh century A.D.. The first century Greek writer Ptolemy refers to the city as Makoraba.

**[0:06:22]** ‘Kaabah’, the cube shaped structure in the center of the most sacred mosque in Mecca, is considered by Muslims as ‘Baitullah’, the house of God. During ancient times, before the advent of Islam, various tribes of the Arabian Peninsula would congregate to worship idols installed inside the ‘Kaabah’. These were removed by Prophet Muhammad, the messenger of Islam, who lived from 570 to 632 A.D.

**[0:06:55]** ‘Hajar-ul-Aswad’, the ‘Black Stone’, is an ancient rock which was venerated since the pre-Islamic times. It was set on the eastern wall of the ‘Kaabah’ by Prophet Muhammad and is a holy Islamic relic. Muslims aspire to perform ‘Haj’, a pilgrimage to Mecca once during their lifetime. As a part of Islamic ritual, pilgrims circumambulate the ‘Kaabah’ and pay respect to ‘Hajar-ul-Aswad’, the ‘Black Stone’.

**[0:07:27]** The word ‘Haj’ originates from Semitic languages. In Hebrew, it is related to the word ‘Haqqa’ which corresponds to righteousness. In Arabic, it connotes an effort to go for an expedition. Thus ‘Haj’ represents the self-effort to embark on an internal spiritual journey. One day, during their stay in Mecca, why Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana were asleep, they were awakened by Jiwan, a ‘Haji’, who rebuked them for sleeping with the feet, pointing towards the direction of the ‘Kaabah’.

**[0:08:09]** The Arabic word ‘Qibla’ denotes the fixed direction of the ‘Kaabah’. Muslims consider pointing one's feet towards ‘Qibla’ as disrespectful. Guru Nanak, with his rational disposition, reminded Jeevan about the Islamic philosophy of ‘Al-Waasa’ which means the Divine manifests in all directions.

**[0:08:36]** Guru Nanak's hagiographies, the ‘Janamsakhis’ and Bhai Gurdas’s ‘Vaar’ mention that when Jiwan moved Guru Nanak’s legs in anger, the ‘Kaabah’ followed the same direction. Interestingly, a narrative involving the ‘Kaabah’ moving is also associated with Hazrat Rabiʿa al-Basri, a female Muslim Sufi Saint.

**[0:09:05]** In my humble view, the narrative of the physical movement of the ‘Kaabah’ is a metaphor for Omnipresence. At Mecca, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana reunited with Makhdum Bahavadi of Multan and Makhdum Haji Abdul Sheikh Bukhari of Uch, who had parted at Shikarpur in Sindh and taken the land route to reach Mecca.

**[0:09:35]** White camping together with the Makhdums of Multan and Uch, some ‘Hajis’ asked Guru Nanak about his religious affiliations. in reply, Guru Nanak sang. Guru Nanak endorses the philosophy of love for humanity

**[0:11:09]** over religious associations. Qazi Rukn-ud-din, the arbiter of the ‘Sharia’, the Islamic Court in Mecca, expressed that the Islamic path is the most superior and invited Guru Nanak to embrace Muslim protocols. In response, In response, Guru Nanak sang.

**[0:12:46]** Guru Nanak expressed the importance of virtuous living over mere adherence to the ritualism. Speaking metaphorically, he told the Qazi he revere compassion as a mosque, spread trust like spreading of the prayer mat, acquire righteous earnings as the teaching of the Quranic scripture, imbibe modesty as circumcision, savour pure intentions as fasting and let positive deeds be the ‘Kaabah’.

**[0:13:20]** In the footsteps of Guru Nanak, having been exposed to diverse thoughts, I often ponder, concepts that are considered important at a time, were not significant in the past and will transform in the future. Guru Nanak says, in this cosmic arena, everything is subject to evolution.

**[0:14:30]** Traveling on the desert route, from Mecca, Guru Nanak, and Bhai Mardana headed north to Medina. Medina, also known as Al-Madinah, which means the luminous city in Arabic, is considered after Mecca as the second holiest site by Muslims.

**[0:15:09]** It is a desert oasis in western Saudi Arabia surrounded by the Hejaz Mountains and volcanic hills. Before the advent of Islam, it was known as Yathrib. In the city center is the mosque Masjid Al-Nabawi, where Prophet Muhammad, the messenger of Islam is buried.

**[0:15:35]** For Muslims, it is an important place of pilgrimage. Religious segregation is a social phenomenon. For ages, non-Muslims have not been allowed to visit the city of Mecca and the Masjid Al-Nabawi in Medina.

**[0:16:02]** Guru Nanak, the epitome of Oneness, was beyond the boundaries of religious mandates. As he did not proclaim alliance to any sect, he would consciously dress in a manner that allowed him to assimilate with natives. This facilitated better communication. Since the news of Guru Nanak's visit to Mecca had travelled ahead, Bhai Mardana, who was a Muslim by faith, anticipated obstacles ahead.

**[0:16:32]** He visited the Masjid Al-Nabawi at Medina to gauge the situation. Thereafter, he took Guru Nanak with him to visit the site to interact with the congregations. Guru Nanak, an advocate of Oneness, saw restrictions and penalties for access to religious sites as discrimination against humanity.

**[0:17:15]** During the course of our journey, our team, comprising of people from diverse faiths, encountered restrictions to access places of worship because of their respective beliefs. At Hindu sites, Muslim members of our team were not permitted, similar to non-Muslims not being permitted to visit Mecca and Medina. Unfortunately, society remains fragmented despite the high ideals of enlightened beings who inspire and practice the unity of humanity.

**[0:17:47]** I am reminded of Guru Nanak verse, From Medina,

**[0:18:35]** Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana took the desert caravan route to Najaf. This route was developed for pilgrims by Begum Zubaida, wife of Caliph Haroon Rashid. We fly from Saudi Arabia to Iraq and resume tracing Guru Nanak's footsteps from Najaf.

**[0:19:10]** The city of Najaf is located approximately 30 kilometers south of the ancient city of Babylon. This city was established in 791 A.D. by Abbasid Caliph Haroon Rashid in memory of Prophet Muhammad's son-in-law, Hazrat Ali, who is buried at the Imam Ali Mosque. This grand structure with a gold gilded dome is a center of pilgrimage for Muslims who consider it as the third holiest Islamic site.

**[0:19:54]** Wadi-us-Salaam, meaning the Valley of Peace in Arabic, is the largest cemetery in the world. Members of the Shia community from across the world aspire to be buried in this cemetery, which is close to the Mausoleum of Imam Ali. It is believed, on the day of judgment, the ones buried here will be raised along with their spiritual leader.

**[0:20:25]** I reflect that humans worry to secure a place in heaven after death and forget the importance of living a meaningful life. Whether cremated or buried,

**[0:21:23]** the body eventually becomes mud, which reaches the potter's urn to create pots and bricks for the houses of people of any belief system. The human body discriminates only when alive. From Najaf, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana proceeded to Karbala, which is at a distance of 75 kilometers.

**[0:21:51]** In tracing Guru Nanak’s footsteps, we travel from Najaf to Karbala. Located on the west of the River Euphrates, Karbala is a city in central Iraq to the southwest of Baghdad.

**[0:22:30]** We are in the holy city of Karbala, the martyrdom and the resting place of Imam Hussain, the grandson of Prophet Muhammad. Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana, on their way to Baghdad, passed through the city of Karbala. The famous battle of Karbala which took place in 680 A.D.

**[0:22:54]** is an important incident in Islamic history. Its outcome resulted in the relationship between the two most prominent sects of Islam, the Shiites and the Sunnis. Minute details of the Battle of Karbala became the hallmark of the Shiite faith and culture. Pilgrims visit the tomb of Karbala to observe 'Ashura', which marks the martyrdom of Imam Hussain and his supporters during the Battle of Karbala.

**[0:23:29]** The sacrifice of Imam Hussain is a testament of his deeply held objective of establishing the foundations of a holistic character of justice and freedom over tyranny and subjugation. In reverence for the martyrs, devotees indulge in self-mutilation rituals, lamenting and cursing the slayers.

**[0:24:26]** A true is homage to martyrdom is achieved by mutilating ego, lamenting for love and cursing disrespect for self and others. Guru Nanak says, the one who lives in the will of Almighty and is merciful, is worthy of being a Muslim. From Karbala, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana travelled on the desert route and reached Baghdad.

**[0:24:59]** In tracing Guru Nanak’s footsteps, we travel from Karbala to Baghdad. Iraq is historically known as a cradle of civilizations.

**[0:25:24]** Its capital, Baghdad, is nestled between the two rivers, Tigris and Euphrates. Ibn Battuta, the 13th century traveller mentions that the travel route from Najaf to Baghdad was a difficult path. Historic texts mention that Guru Nanak took the same path.

**[0:25:53]** Bhai Gurdas writes in his ‘Vaar’ (poetry) that on reaching Baghdad, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana rested at a place outside the city. During Guru Nanak's visit to Baghdad, the city was a part of the Persian Empire under the Iranian Shiite ruler, Shah Ismail of the Safavid dynasty.

**[0:26:29]** In Islamic history, this period is considered a turbulent one due to the increased conflicts between Shiite and Sunni Muslims as each sect had started asserting their political and religious authority. Reflecting on the divisions within faiths, I am reminded of Guru Nanak's verse. Guru Nanak says

**[0:28:15]** associating oneself with a religious sect is easy. The real adherence is in imbibing the essence of the teachings of the faith. In Baghdad, we visit the seat of the Islamic Sufi 'Qadri' tradition. We are at the mausoleum of Pir Mohiuddin Abdul Qadir Gilani Sahib in Baghdad.

**[0:28:56]** He was the founder of the ‘Qadri’ sect. When Guru Nanak visited the city, then, Pir Abdul Rehman was the spiritual leader of the shrine. He was known as ‘Pir Dastgir’, the one who holds the hands for spiritual guidance. One day, during their stay in Baghdad, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana, while in awe of divinity, were singing a verse which propounded the message of Omnipresence.

**[0:29:37]** They expressed that even though the ‘Vedas’, the Hindu scriptures, say that one can get weary of counting numerous worlds, ‘Katebs’, the Islamic scriptures, state that there are 18,000 worlds, but there is only one Omnipresent entity and the rest is its manifestation.

**[0:31:32]** Music was considered a taboo by some Islamic religious groups of this region. Perturbed with the blasphemous act, some people complained to Pir Abdul Rehman, the ‘Pir Dastgir’, who then personally visited Guru Nanak. The two engaged in spiritual dialogue, after which ‘Pir Dastgir’ invited Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana to spent some time with the congregation at the Shrine of Pir Mohiuddin Abdul Qadir Gilani.

**[0:32:06]** At this shrine, Guru Nanak met Faqir Bahlool, the spiritual successor of Bahlool Dana, the eighth century spiritual saint from Kufa. Faqir Bahlool invited Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana to stay with him at his humble abode in the outskirts of Baghdad city. We now visit the mausoleum of Faqir Bahlool.

**[0:32:55]** We are at the site which was once the operational gurudwara in Baghdad, built in the memory of Guru Nanak's visit as well as his meeting with Sheikh Bahlool. This site is close to the Tigris River and the Baghdad Railway station. Punjabi soldiers of the British Indian Army, posted in Baghdad in the early 20th century, built a memorial, adjacent to Faqir Bahlool’s Mausoleum to commemorate Guru Nanak's visit.

**[0:33:42]** Due to the political unrest in Iraq, expats left the country and the gurdwara building was thereafter destroyed in the 2003 Iraq war. We meet Abu Yousuf, whose grandfather was a caretaker of the gurdwara when it was operational. This is the platform on which, I am told,

**[0:34:44]** the Guru Granth Sahib, used to be enshrined and behind us, on this wall, there used to be a plaque, on which was described the history of this place. Good times and challenging times are all transient. Experiencing Abu Yousuf’s warmth and his love for Guru Nanak, I have hope that Guru Nanak's memories which have been erased due to the turbulent times will be re-established when peace returns in this region.

**[0:35:52]** From Baghdad, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana travelled north-east by land to Tabriz. We travel from Iraq to Iran and resume tracing Guru Nanak's footsteps from Tabriz. Iran is bordered to the north-west by Armenia and Azerbaijan, to the north by the Caspian Sea,

**[0:36:34]** to the north-east by Turkmenistan, to east by Afghanistan and to the south-east by Pakistan. In the 16th century, the commonly traversed route from Baghdad to Tehran was through the city of Tabriz in East Azerbaijan province of north-western Iran. The Moroccan explorer Ibn Battuta who travelled to this region a century before Guru Nanak, wrote that Tabriz was then the capital town of the Mongols and was a commercial hub as it was located on the Silk Road.

**[0:37:16]** At the time when Guru Nanak travelled through this region, it was known as Persia. The people of this region at that time, as much as today, practice Islam. In Tabriz, we were unable to find any physical memories associated with Guru Nanak's visit. In the 19th century, a syncretic community of Guru Nanak's adherents from Multan and Sindh in Pakistan had settled in this region as traders.

**[0:37:53]** They would hold spiritual congregations at the Ateshgah, the Fire Temple in the city of Baku in Azerbaijan, a site which was also revered by the Zoroastrians. On the outer walls of the Ateshgah Temple, Guru Nanak’s verses are inscribed along with Vedic Mantras.

**[0:38:20]** These inscriptions were commissioned by the ‘Udasin’ (ascetic) Bawa Bankey Sahib. From Tabriz, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana travelled to Tehran. In tracing Guru Nanak’s footsteps, we travel from Tabriz to Tehran.

**[0:38:57]** On reaching Tehran, we visit the gurdwara, which was established in 1941 A.D. by the adherents of Guru Nanak. Iran once had a sizeable Sikh population in the cities of Tehran and Zahedan.

**[0:39:22]** A community that was sizable, due to the recent political problems in the region has now reduced to a few hundreds. The Iranians call this gurdwara, ‘Masjid-e-Hindan’, the mosque of the people of Hind (India). Reflecting on the amalgamation of the terms gurdwara and masjid, I am reminded of a verse of Guru Nanak in which he says that there are different ways of religious calls from diverse places of worships but only those who listen and imbibe the divinity

**[0:40:04]** are the virtuous. Guru Nanak being intuitively multilingual,

**[0:41:01]** incorporating vernacular dialects of various regions to connect with the local people. In Iran, he sang a verse composed of Persian words. In pursuit of finding happiness,

**[0:42:26]** humans indulge in greed and evil intentions. In humility, Guru Nanak says, he finds happiness in the feet of those who are inclined towards divinity. From Tehran, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana travelled to Mashhad. In tracing Guru Nanak’s footsteps, we travel by train from Tehran to Mashhad.

**[0:42:58]** Ibn Battuta, Marco Polo, Nadir Shah and Alexander, all travelled through these lands, for the purpose of the expansion of their territories or to trade, and the world has heard many and narratives about them. Yet, the world is unaware about the travels of Guru Nanak through these lands, while his mission was, essentially to spread humanity and the Oneness of mankind.

**[0:43:39]** Mashhad is a city located in the north-east of Iran. It is the capital of Razavi Khorasan Province and is a center of religious importance for Shiite Muslims. In reverence of the martyrdom of Imam Reza, the eighth Shia Imam, who died here in 818 A.D., the city of Sanabad was renamed to Mushhad, meaning the place of martyrdom.

**[0:44:09]** At Mashhad, we visit the shrine of Imam Reza. According to ‘Bhai Mani Singh Janamsakhi’, in Mashhad, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana met Pir Abdul Rahman who was visiting the shrine on a pilgrimage from Gardez, a city in Afghanistan.

**[0:44:38]** Impressed by Guru Nanak's ideology of attaching oneself to virtues as opposed to a religious sect, he was curious to know who was more blessed, the Sunnis, who believe only in the Prophet Muhammad, are the Shias, who also have faith in Hazrat Ali. Giving examples from the Islamic code of conduct, Guru Nanak replied, for him, all are equal as the Divine light resides in everyone.

**[0:45:12]** Every soul plays a role in impacting his or her surroundings. The enlightened ones are guiding forces to show us how to pave our own chosen path to divinity. Guru Nanak’s message was that while the five daily prayers

**[0:46:49]** are adhered to by people according to the tenets of Islam, however, a devout Muslim who is true to the faith and truly revers and honors it's mentor, also imbibes the five essences, truthfulness, piety, unity, humility and contemplation.

**[0:47:14]** Historically, Iran's rich culture has been a source of inspiration and influence for many regions. I am an Iranian who has had the opportunity to spend many years in India and in those many years, I had the opportunity to come across the teachings of different faiths, including Sikhism, which was the most impressive.

**[0:47:44]** The teachings of Guru Nanak ji, who travelled so extensively at those times. The beauty of Guru Nanak Ji was, he used the Persian to beautify his teachings, which I really appreciate. Reflecting on the present global politics, which has isolated the country, I am reminded of Guru Nanak's message that the human mind in its whirlpool of egoism, becomes incapable of distinguishing between the attributes which benefit or harm society.

<a id="yt-22"></a>
## Episode 22: Lihaaze Insaniyat — *Respect for Humanity*

*[Watch](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=fatBiRe_TTc) · runtime 67:17*

**[0:00:04]** ‘Lihaaze Insaniyat’, ‘Respect for Humanity’, presents imprints of Guru Nanak’s spiritual legacy in ‘Khorasan’. At ‘Ghandhar’, the gracious humanist spreads the scent of humanity. Guru Nanak says,

**[0:03:27]** for the one engrossed in the materialistic realm, speaking and listening to words of wisdom to embrace Oneness remain transient like a gust of wind. Disciplinary acts to control the mind are worthless if ‘Shaitan’, the internal satanic vices are not kept in check. The oldest ‘Janamsakhis’ (hagiographies) do not present a specific route taken by Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana from Iran to Afghanistan.

**[0:04:03]** Dr. Kirpal Singh in his book, "Janamsakhi Tradition – An Analytical Study" is of the opinion that from Mashhad, Guru Nanak & Bhai Mardana travelled through Balkh and Mazar-i-Sharif in North Afghanistan to reach Kabul. The ‘Bhai Mani Singh Janamsakhi’ mentions that from Mashhad, they travelled through Kandahar in South Afghanistan.

**[0:04:32]** As there are narratives associated with Guru Nanak in Southern Afghanistan and not in the region of Balkh and Mazar-i-Sharif, hence, we trace Guru Nanak's footsteps through Kandahar. From Mashhad, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana travelled through the cities of Kandahar, Ghazni, Kabul and Jalalabad.

**[0:04:56]** Thereafter, travelling through Kurram region, they crossed the Kurram Pass and reached Parachinar. They continued their journey through Ibrahimzai, Peshawar, Eminabad and reached Talwandi, which is now known as Nankana Sahib. From Mashhad, they first visited Kandahar. Tracing Guru Nanak's footsteps, we travel from Iran to Afghanistan and resume our journey from Kandahar.

**[0:05:49]** Kandahar, the second largest city of Afghanistan, is located on the banks of the Arghandab River, at the elevation of 3310 feet. Kandahar derives its name from ‘Gandhar’. It is strategically located on the trade route that connects Middle-East and Central Asia with the Indian sub-continent.

**[0:06:22]** ‘Gandhar’ is a Sanskrit word, meaning a land of aroma, probably referring to its population, which comprise a mélange of cultures. Kandahar was founded by Alexander, the Greek King, and was later ruled by the Indo Gangetic rulers of the Mauryan Empire and the Buddhist Emperor Ashoka.

**[0:06:46]** The Central Asian and the Middle Eastern rulers introduced the Islamic culture to this region. In Kandahar, we visit the mausoleum of Sufi mystic Baba Wali Kandahari, whose association with Guru Nanak finds mention in the narrative at Panja Sahib Gurdwara in the city of Hassan Abdal in Pakistan.

**[0:07:26]** The ‘Bhai Mani Singh Janamsakhi’ records that at Kandahar, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana were hosted by Baba Wali Kandahari. Some ‘Janamsakhi’ (hagiography) art that reflect this meeting in Kandahar depict Shah Sharaf seated with Guru Nanak and Baba Wali Kandahari. While it cannot be said with certainty, it is possible that the Shah Sharaf portrayed in the art was Sheikh Tatihar, the then spiritual head of Bu Ali Qalandar Shah Sharaf’s shrine, with whom Guru Nanak had earlier interacted during his visit to Panipat in his first odyssey.

**[0:08:14]** According to an oral narrative, at the time of Guru Nanak's visit, the governor of Kandahar was an unjust and arrogant administrator. He would visit the mosque five times a day to perform prayers. Unfair behaviour, however, can’t be compensated by performing obligatory religious rituals. Hence, Guru Nanak sang to convey a profound message.

**[0:09:54]** An elephant is focussed on consuming large quantities of food and it wiles away time in belching and scattering dust or wood itself. Guru Nanak analogizes that similarly a mind consumed with ego, belches away precious moments while being mired in self-conceit. He says, a delightful personality is attained through eradicating one’s ego.

**[0:10:24]** Decades of political unrest in Afghanistan has impacted its socio-religious diversity. The ethnic community of ‘Nanakpanthis’, the Hindu and Sikh followers of Guru Nanak, who were once an integral part of Afghan culture, has been compelled to seek asylum in new lands since the early 1990s. We visit Dr. Raghunath, an ardent follower of Guru Nanak.

**[0:10:53]** Against all odds, he has chosen not to leave Afghanistan and is the last 'Nanakpanthi' (adherents of Guru Nanak) resident of Kandahar. Reflecting on the pain inflicted on the natives

**[0:12:03]** due to political conditions in their motherland, I am reminded of Guru Nanak’s verse. Guru Nanak says, the world is suffering from the malady of negativity.

**[0:12:35]** The medicine for this illness is self reflection. Similar to ‘like cures like’, introspection becomes the means of self-healing. Dr. Raghunath takes us to a site, built in memory of Guru Nanak, which is being maintained by him single handedly as the rest of the 'Nanakpanthi' community has left the country. This 'Dharamsal' (place of worship) was the first one to be established in Kandahar.

**[0:13:13]** There were five gurdwaras established in Kandahar, of which only one is left operational. During Guru Nanak's visit to Kandahar, like present times, this region was going through a turbulent period because of invasions by the Mughal Emperor Babur.

**[0:13:59]** According to an oral tradition, witnessing the plight of the people, Guru Nanak is said to have sang. Guru Nanak says,

**[0:15:17]** human vice, the desire for power, wealth, beauty and social status have plundered nature's creation. True in present times, the centuries old 'Great Game' is still being played in the lands of Afghanistan for geo-strategic and socio-political authority. It has resulted in loss of lives and culture.

**[0:15:47]** From Kandahar, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana travelled to Ghazni. Tracing Guru Nanak's footsteps, we travel from Kandahar to Ghazni. Ghazni means a jewel.

**[0:16:23]** The city of Ghazni, in central Afghanistan, falls on the ancient trade and transit route, which is the reason for its present multi-ethnicity. Guru Nanak, on his journey from Kandahar to Kabul, passed through the city of Ghazni. The historical route connecting Kandahar and Kabul passed through Ghazni.

**[0:16:51]** The oldest ‘Janamsakhi’ texts do not record any event of Guru Nanak in Ghazni. However, according to various analytical studies, historians are of the opinion that Guru Nanak would have had to travel through this city to reach Kabul. At Ghazni, we visit the Nand Lal Goya Gurdwara, which is in the heart of the city.

**[0:17:30]** Guru Nanak's philosophy was accepted by the locals in Ghazni. Presently, there are four gurdwaras in this town. We are standing at the gurdwara made in the memory of Bhai Nand Lal Goya, who spent his time with the 10th Nanak, Guru Gobind Singh. The city of Ghazni, once had a substantial population of ‘Nanakpanthis’, the Hindu and Sikh followers of Guru Nanak, who have left Afghanistan due to political uncertainty.

**[0:18:06]** The community had built four gurdwaras for its congregation. In present times, services are only conducted at the Nand Lal Goya Gurdwara by the minuscule community. All other sites, while closed for services, are being well maintained through funds sent by the Afghani Sikh diaspora. From Ghazni, we make a detour from Guru Nanak’s footsteps to visit remote areas, where once lived the followers of Guru Nanak, who in present times have abandoned their ancestral lands.

**[0:18:51]** Heading up north along the Afghanistan-Pakistan border, we make a detour to a town named Khost. At Khost, we visit a residential area named Prem Nagar, which was once thriving with ‘Nanakpanthi’ Hindu and Sikh adherents of Guru Nanak.

**[0:19:24]** This gurudwara in Khost was built by the ‘Nanakpanthi’ community. It was destroyed in the political turmoil that Afghanistan experienced. Four to five Hindu and Sikh families residing in Khost maintain a room within the precincts of this damaged gurudwara, which serves as a syncretic place of worship.

**[0:19:53]** Here, the Guru Granth Sahib, the Sikh Scripture is enshrined along with idols of Hindu deities and ‘Om Har Shri Nath Ji’ is inscribed on the walls. During our travels, we have seen many syncretic sites across Afghanistan and Pakistan.

**[0:20:20]** They reflect the erstwhile intertwined belief system of the Indus belt, where Guru Nanak's philosophy was the fulcrum. This tradition has been on a decline since the last century. Khalida Begum, alias Padmey, an elderly Muslim Afghani resident of Prem Nagar, is an iron lady who is taking care of this gurdwara to ensure it does not fall in the hands of land grabbers.

**[0:21:27]** According to her, we were her special guests, the adherents of her beloved Guru Nanak. Khalida Begum's dynamic personality, a combination of feminine softness and masculine fortitude was remarkable. I reflect on how gender roles, biases, patriarchy and female oppression has corrupted the social-cultural equilibrium in society.

**[0:21:57]** Contrary to accepted gender roles, when a male unravels his feminine aspects and the female her masculine aspects, it empowers them to achieve their maximum potential as humans. Guru Nanak says, in accord with the laws of creation, we are a composite of feminine and masculine energies.

**[0:22:42]** From Khost, we make a detour to Gardez, the capital of the Paktia Province. Located within a huge alpine valley in the Sulaiman Mountains of eastern Afghanistan, this region has had a rich history of Hinduism and Buddhism and is now Islamic.

**[0:23:25]** It is stated in the ‘Bhai Mani Singh Janamsakhi’ that at the shrine of Imam Reza in Mashhad in Iran, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana had interacted with pir Abdul Rahman, who was a pilgrim from this city of Gardez. We are standing at the site where once existed the Gardez Gurdwara.

**[0:24:02]** This gurdwara got destroyed in the political unrest in Afghanistan. The Gardez based ‘Nanakpanthi’ community has now dispersed from the city. Ikram-ud-din guided us to a site

**[0:25:15]** which the locals associate with Guru Nanak. Close to the Gardez Gurdwara is this water spring which is known as ‘Chashma Merab’. The locals here believe that Guru Nanak visited this site.

**[0:25:40]** However, the ‘Janamsakhi’ texts do not mention any narrative of Guru Nanak in Gardez. Companion planting is an age old technique, wherein two different species of plants are planted next to each other, such that both are encouraged to thrive. Humans too thrive in diverse company.

**[0:26:05]** Ikram-ud-Din’s yearning for his Sikh and Hindu brethren endorses this theory. Polarization in society is depriving our progeny of the joyous virtues of diversity in Oneness. Guru Nanak says, when the mind cherishes Oneness, it celebrates in joy.

**[0:26:48]** from Ghazni, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana travelled to Kabul. In Guru Nanak's footsteps, we resume our journey from Ghazni to Kabul. Kabul is the capital city of Afghanistan, which is nestled in a bowl shaped valley.

**[0:27:32]** In different eras, it has hosted various cultures and religions like Buddhism, Hinduism, Zoroastrianism, Judaism. Presently, Kabul is predominantly Islamic. Historically, Kabul was once a center of Vedic culture.

**[0:28:01]** During Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana’s visit to Afghanistan, it came under the control of Babur, the founder of the Mughal Empire. At Asamai Hill in Kabul, is an ancient temple dedicated to the feminine Hindu deity, ‘Asamai’. ‘Asa’ means hope ‘Mai’ means mother.

**[0:28:31]** Venerated as the goddess of hope, for centuries, the ‘Asamai’ Temple was a place for congregation, probably a reason for Guru Nanak to visit this site so that he could interact with spiritualists. According to the narrative in the ‘Bhai Mani Singh Janamsakhi’, in Kabul, on his way to ‘Asamai’ Hill, Guru Nanak met some holy men.

**[0:29:00]** Seeing Guru Nanak's attire, the holy men were not able to identify his religious disposition. The Hindu holy men cautioned him for visiting a Hindu site in the land of an Islamic ruler. The Muslim holy men rebuked him as a ‘Kafir’, a non-believer, for visiting a non-Islamic place of worship.

**[0:29:27]** In response, Guru Nanak sang. Guru Nanak,

**[0:30:35]** in his verse, uses the term Allah which is an Arabic word referring to the Creator. He explains that we humans might address the Creator with different names, associate it with different religions or venerate it in different sites but it is the One who remains Omnipresent.

**[0:31:02]** Some ‘Janamsakhi’ art depict Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana being hosted by a local resident of Kabul, named Man Chand. After Guru Nanak’s visit to Kabul, many ethnic Afghanis started adopting his philosophy, which continued to spread through ‘Dharamshals’, ‘Udasins’, ‘Nanakpanthis’ and the ‘Khalsa’ Sikhs.

**[0:31:47]** Before the Afghan Civil War of 1992, the ‘Nanakpanthi’ population, comprising of Sikhs and Hindus, the believers of Guru Nanak are estimated to have been around 250,000. In present times, less than a thousand remain in Afghanistan. The affluence of the erstwhile community, before its migration from Kabul, can be ascertained from the empty premises of many gurdwara buildings across the city.

**[0:32:29]** We visit Gurdwara Baba Sri Chand. It is believed this is the first site made in the memory of Guru Nanak's visit to Kabul. It was built by Baba Almast, an ‘Udasin’ (ascetic) preacher of Guru Nanak's philosophy. As per ‘Mahan Kosh’ by Kahan Singh Nabha, Baba Almast was born in a Gaur Kashmiri family in 1553 and died in 1643 A.D.

**[0:33:05]** This shrine, in the heart of Kabul City, in Shor Bazaar, is made by the ‘Udasin’ (ascetic) followers. There is space given to Guru Nanak, Baba Sri Chand and Hindu Gods and Goddesses. The shrine is now being maintained by a handful of Sikhs and ‘Nanakpanthis’, who are still residing in Kabul.

**[0:33:33]** It is believed that Baba Sri Chand also visited Kabul and was instrumental in spreading the message of Guru Nanak. We now visit Paen Chowk in Kabul city. At the site of this commercial building in Paen Chowk, once existed, the earliest gurdwara made in the memory of Guru Nanak's visit to Kabul.

**[0:34:11]** The gurdwara and a mosque adjacent to it were acquired in the redevelopment of this area. We now visit Gurdwara Karte Parwan, which in present times is the main congregation site for the ‘Nanakpanthi’, Sikh and Hindu community of Kabul.

**[0:34:59]** Locals mentioned that before the political problems of Afghanistan in 1992, Afghani Muslims who embraced Guru Nanak's philosophy would visit the gurdwaras. After much effort, we were able to connect with Ustad Lali, descendant of Ustad Rahim Baksh, an Afghani Muslim singer who would devotedly sing verses from the Guru Granth Sahib, the Sikh scripture.

**[0:37:23]** From Kabul, Guru Nanak and by Mardana travelled to Jalalabad. In Guru Nanak's footsteps we travel from Kabul to Jalalabad. Located at the junction of the Kabul River and the Kunar River, in a plateau to the south of the Hindukush mountains, Jalalabad is the capital city of Nangarhar province in East Afghanistan.

**[0:38:12]** Linked by highways to the Khyber Pass and the Torkham border with Pakistan, it is a leading center for social and trading activity. The 630 A.D., famous Buddhist monk, Xuan Zang, has mentioned the name of this city as Adinapur, which was then a center of Greco-Buddhist culture.

**[0:38:43]** In the city of Jalalabad, we visit the Guru Nanak Darbar Gurdwara. On reaching Jalalabad, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana made their base on the banks of River Landa, which is now known as River Kabul.

**[0:39:07]** The river has shifted its course now. The Guru Nanak Durbar is made in the memory of Guru Nanak's visit to Jalalabad. The Sikh community of Jalalabad guided us to the nearby village of Sultanpur to a site built in the memory of Guru Nanak's visit.

**[0:39:36]** In close proximity to Sultanpur, we stopped at the Dargah of Pir Rattan Nath in Charbagh village. Pir Rattan Nath lived much before Guru Nanak.

**[0:40:04]** A native of Nepal, Pir Rattan Nath was a follower of Guru Gorakhnath, the 10th century spiritual leader. Known as Rattan Nath by Hindus and Haji Rattan by Muslims, he is revered by people of both faiths, who collectively established a ‘Nath Jogi’ (ascetic) commune at this site to commemorate his influence in Afghanistan.

**[0:40:31]** In his journeys, Guru Nanak had interacted with communes of ‘Gorakhpanthi Nath Jogis’ (ascetics) in various regions. Interestingly, at many such places, sites are dedicated in memory of Guru Nanak in close proximity to the site associate with the ‘Nath’ (ascetic) sect. We proceed further to the site dedicated to Guru Nanak at the nearby village of Sultanpur.

**[0:41:14]** In the outskirts of Jalalabad, on the Surkh Rōd in village Sultanpur, the locals here believe that this water spring was unveiled by Guru Nanak in this dry and arid region. The locals here now call it as ‘Chashma Sahib’. The earliest ‘Janamsakhi’ texts do not mention this spring.

**[0:41:41]** In the oral tradition, the unveiling of water springs in many water scarce regions is often associated with narratives about Guru Nanak. In my humble opinion, this is a metaphor which represents the flowing of spiritual wisdom to quench the thirst of the arid mind. Though society has evolved, yet the mind continues to remain arid on many fronts.

**[0:42:14]** Even today, the arid mind continues to discriminate against women, a social wise against which Guru Nanak had raised his voice and created awareness in the 16th century. Guru Nanak says,

**[0:43:11]** women create future generations, give birth to meritorious beings, play an intrinsic role in every aspect of society, yet, they continue to be subjected to discrimination. Guru Nanak was much ahead of his time for voicing his thoughts on social issues.

**[0:43:35]** He strongly discredited gender inequality. From Jalalabad, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana travelled through Kurram region, a geographic area which is divided by the Durrand Line between Afghanistan and Pakistan.

**[0:44:04]** In the 15th century, the travel route from Kabul to Peshawar was through the Kurram Pass. We visit Babu Khel village at Chamkani in Kurram region of Afghanistan, which till a few years back was bustling with the followers of Guru Nanak. We are in Babu Khel, a short distance from the Afghanistan-Pakistan border.

**[0:44:45]** There are two narratives about the place of Bhai Mardana’s passing away. In ‘Bala Janamsakhi’, which is a more recent text, it is written that Bhai Mardana breathed his last in the Kurram region. Some versions of the ‘Bala Janamasakhi’ art show that Bhai Mardana’s body was put to rest on a pyre, while others show a tomb, indicating that he was buried.

**[0:45:13]** However, the earlier ‘Janamsakhi’ (hagiography) texts state that he passed away in Kartarpur. In accordance with the "Janamsakhi Tradition, An Analytical Study" written by Dr. Kirpal Singh, we continue with the narrative of Bhai Mardana traveling further with Guru Nanak. This Gurdwara Thara Sahib was built by the ‘Nanakpanthi’ community of Babu Khel in the memory of Guru Nanak's visit through this region.

**[0:45:55]** It is the last gurdwara that falls on the path of Guru Nanak's travels through Afghanistan. There is no physical sight in the Kurram region associated with Bhai Mardana’s passing away. Once in a year, the community from across Afghanistan comes together at this Gurdwara Thara Sahib to keep alive the memories of the past.

**[0:46:26]** Guru Nanak mentions that life and death is dependent on just one breath. Being unaware of when one’s last breath will occur, one should strive to utilize each breath to the best use. From Kurram region

**[0:47:48]** From Kurram region, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana travelled to Parachinar. We fly from Afghanistan to Pakistan and resume our travels in Guru Nanak's footsteps from Parachinar in Kurram region. The valley of Parachinar in Pakistan is close to

**[0:48:34]** the Pakistan-Afghanistan border. The name Parachinar is derived from ‘Para’, attributed to one of the tribes of the valley and 'Chinar' to the Maple trees which grow in abundance in this region. The ‘Nanakpanthi’, Hindu and Sikh population of this region shrank significantly after the partition of 1947.

**[0:49:04]** Later, the political unrest in this border region compelled the remaining community to move to safer areas. This led to abandonment of many sites which were built to commemorate Guru Nanak’s visit to this region. In present times, on a few occasions every year, the followers of Guru Nanak from the Khyber Pakhtunkhwa region of Pakistan congregate in Parachinar to perform religious services.

**[0:49:40]** In Parachinar, we first visit Malikhel Village. On the way from Afghanistan to Peshawar, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana took the way through the Kurram Pass.

**[0:50:07]** This gurdwara at Mali Khel was built in the memory of the site where they made the first stop in Parachinar. We now visit Malana in Parachinar. In the valley of Parachinar,

**[0:50:51]** from Mali Khel, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana reached Malana. This Gurdwara Talla Sahib was made in the memory of their visit. This gurdwara is on a hilltop in the valley which is surrounded on the three sides by Afghanistan border. From Parachinar, on route to Peshawar, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana travelled through Ibrahimzai, a village close to the city of Kohat.

**[0:51:24]** From Malana in Parachinar, we make a stop at Ibrahimzai to visit the site built to commemorate Guru Nanak's visit in this region. While the followers of Guru Nanak no longer reside in these regions, this gurdwara has been resurrected in recent years by the Peshawar based Sikh community who now hold annual congregations at this site.

**[0:52:03]** From Ibrahimzai, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana travelled to Peshawar. In tracing Guru Nanak’s footsteps, we travel to the valley of Peshawar. Peshawar is the capital of the Pakistani province of Khyber Pakhtunkhwa.

**[0:52:40]** Its population is predominantly of Pashtun descent. At the time of Guru Nanak's visit, this city was known as Parashawar, a Sanskrit word meaning, the city of men. In the era of Emperor Akbar, it was renamed Peshawar. Historically, it was once the gateway of diverse and rich cultures.

**[0:53:06]** In this city, the ancient scholar Panini wrote his book, ‘Astadhyay’ which became the basis for the study of structural linguistics in modern languages. We visit the ancient Shiva Temple at Gorakh Hatri

**[0:54:06]** in the Peshawar City Center. Gorakh Hatri in the center of Peshawar City is an important archeological site where layers of history has existed. It was once an important Buddhist center where Gautama Buddha is believed to have meditated under a 100 feet pipal tree.

**[0:54:38]** Gorakh Hatri is also visually depicted in ‘Baburnama’, the autobiography of Emperor Babur, who invaded the city in the early 16th century. At the time of Guru Nanak, the Gorakh Hatri in Peshawar was a center of influence for the ‘Gorakh Panthi Jogis’ (ascetics).

**[0:55:09]** The ‘Gorakh Panthi Jogis’ (ascetics) were the followers of Guru Gorakhnath who had influence from Nepal, India, Pakistan and till Afghanistan. The center in Peshawar was the stronghold for the North-West Frontier region. Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana also visited this site. When Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana arrived at the Gorakh Hatri temple complex, ‘Bhandara’, community food was being served.

**[0:55:45]** After the commune had finished eating food, ‘Jogis’ (ascetics) enquired if Guru Nanak was a householder or an ascetic. When Guru Nanak replied that he was a householder, one of the ‘Jogis’ (ascetics) commented that a householder engrossed in familial affairs loses the ability to reach the higher state of enlightenment. They are akin to an intoxicated person who loses the ability to focus.

**[0:56:15]** In response, Guru Nanak sang. Spirituality is gained by training the mind to live a simple,

**[0:57:32]** virtuous life and not through self-denial. Guru Nanak says, an ascetic can be detached from society, yet be entangled in pride and lead an egocentric life. While a householder can be involved with social affairs, yet remain humble and lead a spiritual life.

**[0:57:56]** During his stay at Gorakh Hatri, a ‘Jogi’ (ascetic) asked why Guru Nanak was not appreciative of the display of outward garb and occult powers. In response, Guru Nanak sang. Guru Nanak says,

**[0:59:19]** salt and paper remain separate when smeared with butter. Similarly, smeared with contemplation, spiritualists remain detached from distinctive garb and occultism. In humility, they refrain from showcasing intellectual and physical powers as strength lies in demonstrating love.

**[0:59:44]** From Peshawar, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana proceeded towards the east and once again visited the city of Saidpur, which is now known as Eminabad. In Guru Nanak's footsteps, we travel from Peshawar to Eminabad. Bhai Lalo, the carpenter who hosted Guru Nanak during his first visit to Eminabad,

**[1:00:30]** welcomed him again to his home. During Guru Nanak's second visit, the city of Eminabad was in a state of devastation. The Central Asian invader, Emperor Babur, the founder of the Mughal Empire in India, established his rule in Afghanistan and then attacked Punjab.

**[1:01:02]** History records his plundering of the city of Eminabad and his heartless slaughter of the natives who dared to resist. Sparing neither women nor children, whether Hindu or Muslim, he ruthlessly kept them in captivity. In his memoir, the ‘Baburnama’, Babur professes that the residents of the nearby city of Sialkot were spared by his forces as they surrendered without resistance.

**[1:01:48]** Jahandad Khan walked us to the Chakki Sahib Gurdwara which was abandoned in the partition of 1947 and resurrected in 1992. We are at the Chakki Sahib Gurdwara, and this marks the location where Baba Guru Nanak was imprisoned by the Mughal Emperor, Babur, when he invaded the Punjab.

**[1:02:10]** Later, a gurdwara was built here to mark that site, and this is where we are right now. According to the Sikh 'Janamsakhi' (hagiography) traditions, Bhai Mardana and Guru Nanak were among those imprisoned, when Babur's army ransacked Eminabad. The Guru was made to grind grain in imprisonment. Guru Nanak composed four lyrical hymns on the destruction caused by the invasion, which came to be known as the ‘Babur Vani’.

**[1:02:35]** He profoundly described the plunder that was happening, irrespective of faith, cast and religion, by saying, ‘Janamsakhi’ artworks show that after Mughal Emperor Babur's attack and subsequent victory, some people were distraught, while some rejoiced.

**[1:03:06]** Observing the two opposing emotions of society, Guru Nanak sang. Guru Nanak says,

**[1:04:03]** pleasure and pain are created by the laws of nature which are ordained by the One Omnipresent Creator. From Eminabad, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana proceeded to Talwandi, which is now called Nankana Sahib. Tracing Guru Nanak’s footsteps, we travel from Eminabad to Nankana Sahib.

**[1:04:55]** Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana were returning to Talwandi after having travelled for approximately 4 years. The third odyssey to the west, which started in 1520 A.D., is estimated to have concluded in that year 1524 A.D., when Guru Nanak was about 55 years of age. I often wonder, during Guru Nanak’s odysseys, which moments would have been pivotal.

**[1:05:27]** ‘Amrit Vela’, a term in Punjabi, comprises two words, 'Amrit', meaning ambrosia and ‘Vela’, referring to a time of day. Guru Nanak says, that momentous moment when truth, contemplation and uplifting thoughts are imbibed is the ambrosial time.
